Zakat on Mixed Silver Coins (Maghshoosha)
Ruling on zakat for mixed silver coins with proof and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat for mixed silver coins and apply the criteria of dominant silver versus dominant alloy to determine zakat liability.

Core Idea
For zakat, mixed dirhams follow the majority: silver-dominant coins are pure silver; alloy-dominant coins are commodities.

In the time of the early Fuqaha, coins were often debased with base metals. This raised the question: when a coin contains both silver and an alloy, does the zakat obligation apply to the entire coin or only to its silver content? The Hanafi school provides a detailed ruling based on the principle of ghilibah (dominance). This lesson unpacks the ruling from the commentary of Al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two categories
The passage first divides mixed dirhams (maghshoosha) into two categories: those where silver is dominant (ghalib) and those where the alloy is dominant.
The criterion is the majority by weight.
2
Apply the ruling for dominant silver
If silver is dominant, the coin is legally treated as pure silver. The alloy is considered mustahlak (negligible). Zakat is due on the entire coin at the rate for silver (2.5% of the weight, provided the weight reaches the nisab of 200 dirhams).
The proof is that the Shariah obligated zakat on the name 'dirham'.
3
Analyze the case of dominant alloy
When the alloy dominates, the coin is not treated as silver. Instead, it is like a commodity (e.g., satua). Then one looks at its status: if it is current currency (rai'jah) or intended for trade, its market value is assessed. If that value reaches the nisab of the lowest silver dirhams, zakat is due on the value. If it is neither, no zakat is due unless the silver content alone reaches 200 dirhams and can be extracted.
This is similar to the treatment of fulus (copper coins).
4
Consider the extraction condition
The text notes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: some require that the silver be extractable from the alloy for zakat to be due on the silver content. Ghayat al-Bayan states extraction is not a condition; it suffices that the silver is present in the coins to the amount of nisab.
The preferred view is that extraction is not a condition.
5
Note the ruling on ghatarifah
A specific type of coin called ghatarifah is mentioned. One opinion says zakat is due in count (five per two hundred). Another opinion (al-Salaf) says they are treated like fulus: if current or trade, zakat on value; if not, no zakat.
This illustrates regional variations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وغالب الورق ورق لا عكسه يعني أن الدراهم إذا كانت مغشوشة، فإن كان الفضة الغالب هو فهي كالدراهم الخالصة لأن الغش فيها مستهلك لا فرق في مطلقاً الزيوف والنبهرجة، وما غلب فضته على غشه تناوله اسم الدراهم والشرع أوجب باسم الدراهم، فإن غلب الغش فليس كالفضة كالستوقة فينظر إن كانت رائجة أو نوى التجارة اعتبرت قيمتها فإن بلغت نصاباً من أدنى الدراهم التي تجب فيها الزكاة وهي التي غلبت فضتها وجبت فيها الزكاة وإلا فلا. وإن لم تكن أثماناً رائجة ولا منوية للتجارة فلا زكاة فيها إلا أن يكون ما فيها من الفضة يبلغ مائتي درهم بأن كانت كثيرة ويتخلص من الغش لأن الصفر لا تجب الزكاة فيها إلا بنية التجارة والفضة لا يشترط فيها نية التجارة، فإن كان ما فيها لا يتخلص فلا شيء عليه لأن الفضة فيه قد هلكت كذا في كثير من الكتب. وفي غاية البيان: الظاهر أن خلوص الفضة من الدراهم ليس بشرط، بل المعتبر أن تكون في الدراهم فضة بقدر النصاب فأما الغطارفة فقيل : يجب في كل مائتين منها خمسة منها عدداً أعز الأثمان والنقود عندهم. وقال السلف : ينظر إن كانت أثماناً رائجة أو سلعاً للتجارة تجب الزكاة في قيمتها كالفلوس وإن لم تكن للتجارة فلا زكاة فيها لأن ما فيها من الأخرى ليتم أربعة مثاقيل وأربعين درهماً لأن الزكاة لا تجب عنده في الكسور ا هـ .
His statement: (And the majority of silver is silver, not the opposite). Meaning that when dirhams are counterfeit (mixed), if silver is dominant, they are like pure dirhams because the alloy is considered negligible; there is no difference at all between defective coins (zuyuf) and base coins (nubahrja). Whatever has its silver content dominant over its alloy, the name 'dirham' applies to it, and the Law obligated zakat using the name 'dirham'. If the alloy dominates, then it is not like silver, such as 'satua' (a type of base coin). Then it is considered: if they are current as currency or intended for trade, their value is assessed; if that value reaches the nisab of the lowest dirhams that require zakat (i.e., those with dominant silver), then zakat is due; otherwise not. If they are neither current nor intended for trade, there is no zakat on them unless the silver content reaches two hundred dirhams, meaning if they are many and the silver can be extracted from the alloy, because brass does not require zakat except with the intention of trade, while silver does not require trade intention. If the silver cannot be extracted, then nothing is due because the silver is considered ruined. This is stated in many books. In Ghayat al-Bayan: apparently, the extraction of silver from the dirhams is not a condition; rather, the consideration is that there is silver in the dirhams to the amount of the nisab. As for al-Ghatarifah (a type of coin), it is said: for every two hundred of them, five are due in count, as they are the most precious currency among them. And al-Salaf said: if they are current or trade goods, zakat is due on their value like fulus (copper coins); if not for trade, no zakat is due because what is in them of other (metal) is to complete four mithqals and forty dirhams, because zakat is not obligatory on fractions in his view. End quote.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on zakat for mixed silver coins (maghshoosha). The matn states the principle: 'the majority of silver is silver, not the opposite.' The commentary explains: if silver is dominant, the coin is treated as pure silver because the alloy is negligible. The coins are called dirhams, and the Shariah obligated zakat on the name 'dirhams'. If the base metal dominates, the coin is like base metal (e.g., satua). In that case, it is treated as a commodity: if it is current currency or intended for trade, its value is assessed; if that value reaches the nisab of the lowest pure silver dirhams, zakat is due. If it is neither currency nor trade goods, no zakat is due unless the silver content alone reaches 200 dirhams and can be extracted. If extraction is impossible, the silver is considered ruined and no zakat is due. The text then mentions a view from Ghayat al-Bayan that extraction is not a condition; rather, the presence of silver equal to nisab in the coins suffices. It also notes a difference regarding 'ghatarifah' coins: some say count-based zakat applies; al-Salaf say they are valued like fulus. The passage concludes with the condition that if the coins are not trade goods, no zakat is due because the silver content is insufficient to complete the nisab.

Key Terms
مغشوشة (maghshoosha): Coins where silver is mixed with a base metal like copper.
غطارفة (ghatarifah): A type of coin with a specific composition; possibly a regional currency.
رائجة (rai'jah): Current as currency, i.e., accepted as money in the market.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of a mixed coin where the base metal dominates and it is not current nor intended for trade?
A) Zakat is due on the total weight.
B) Zakat is due on the value.
C) No zakat is due unless the silver content reaches nisab and is extractable.
D) Zakat is due in count (five per two hundred).

Key Takeaway
For mixed silver coins, the Hanafi ruling hinges on dominance: if silver dominates, treat as silver; if alloy dominates, treat as a commodity based on value and intention.

The Role of Custom in Determining Zakat Nisab Weight
Ruling on how dirham weight varies by time and place for zakat nisab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the nisab weight of dirhams for zakat follows the local customary weight and explain the evidence from the practice of the Prophet and Umar.

Core Idea
Zakat nisab weight follows local custom, not a fixed historical measure.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi principle: the consideration of local custom ('urf) in determining the nisab weight for zakat on silver. The text from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq demonstrates that the weight of 200 dirhams changed over time, from the Prophet's era to Umar's to later. This shows that the requirement is not a rigid historical standard but adapts to the prevailing currency of each place and time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the nisab weight for zakat on silver? The text discusses how many dirhams constitute the nisab.
The nisab is 200 dirhams, but the weight of each dirham varies.
2
Recognize the textual evidence
The text cites that in the Prophet's time, 200 dirhams weighed 5 mithqals; in Umar's time, 6 mithqals; and later, 7 mithqals. This shows variation.
The weight changed, indicating it is not fixed.
3
Extract the legal principle
The variation proves that custom ('urf) determines the standard. The text states: 'Rather, consideration in every time is the custom of the people of that time.'
Hanafi fiqh gives weight to ‘urf as a subsidiary source.
4
Apply the principle
In any time and place, the local dirham weight is used. So the nisab is 200 dirhams by the local weight.
For example, Ghitarifah dirhams are considered standard if they are the common currency.
5
Note the practical impact
Zakat obligation depends on owning 200 dirhams by local weight, not a fixed historical weight.
A Muslim must check the customary weight in his locality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العشرة منها وزن سبعة مثاقيل وغالب الورق ورق لا عكسه وفي عروض تجارة بلغت لأن كل ذلك بين أنه كان ذلك بين تعريف السجاوندي فهو خلاف الواقع إذ الواقع أن درهم مصر لا يزيد على أربعة وستين شعيرة منه مقدر بأربع خرانيب والخرنوبة مقدرة بأربع قمحات وسط اهـ. وذكر الولوالجي ربع أن الزكاة تجب في الغطارفة إذا كانت مائتين لأنها اليوم من دراهم الناس وإن لم تكن من دراهم الناس في الزمن الأول. وإنما يعتبر في كل زمان عادة أهل ذلك الزمان، ألا ترى أن مقدار المائتين لوجوب الزكاة من الفضة إنما تعتبر بوزن سبعة وإن كان مقدار المائتين في الزكاة في زمن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان بوزن خمسة وفي زمن عمر رضي الله عنه بوزن ستة، فيعتبر دراهم أهل كل بلد بوزنهم و دنانير كل بلد بوزنهم وإن كان الوزن يتفاوت .اهـ.
Ten of them weigh seven mithqals, and the majority of silver is silver, not its opposite. And regarding trade goods, it reached [nisab] because all that is clear. It was like that. The definition of al-Sajawandi contradicts reality, because the reality is that the dirham of Egypt does not exceed sixty-four barleycorns, estimated as four kharinub, and the kharinub is estimated as four medium wheat grains. End. Al-Walwalji mentioned: Zakat is obligatory on Ghitarifah [dirhams] if they reach two hundred, because today they are among the people's dirhams, even if they were not among the people's dirhams in earlier times. Rather, consideration in every time is the custom of the people of that time. Do you not see that the amount of two hundred for the obligation of zakat on silver is considered at the weight of seven, even though the amount of two hundred for zakat in the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him) was at the weight of five, and in the time of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) at the weight of six. So the dirhams of the people of each city are considered by their weight, and the dinars of each city by their weight, even if the weight varies. End.

Commentary
This passage, from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, discusses the basis for determining the nisab of silver for zakat. The author first notes a discrepancy with al-Sajawandi's definition regarding the weight of an Egyptian dirham, but then moves to the core ruling: the custom of each time and place determines the weight of dirhams. Evidence is given that in the Prophet's time, 200 dirhams weighed 5 mithqals; in Umar's time, 6 mithqals; and later, 7 mithqals. Therefore, the nisab weight is not fixed historically but follows the prevalent dirham of each era and region. This is a practical ruling ensuring zakat obligations adjust to local currency standards.

Key Terms
الغطارفة (al-ghitārifa): Dirhams minted under Ghitrif, a governor of Khurasan, which became a common currency in Bukhara.
عرف (‘urf): Customary practice of the people in a given time and place.

Key Takeaway
The nisab weight for zakat on silver is determined by the customary weight of dirhams in each locality and era, not a fixed historical measure.

Ruling on Zakat for Mixed Dirhams
Detailed ruling on zakat for mixed dirhams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for zakat on mixed dirhams and determine when zakat is wajib or not based on silver dominance, circulation, and trade intent.

Core Idea
For mixed dirhams, dominance of silver makes them like pure; otherwise assess market value if currency or trade goods.

The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key distinction in zakat for dirhams that are not pure silver but mixed with alloy. The ruling hinges on which metal dominates (ghalib) and the legal status of the coins. Understanding this distinction is crucial to avoid overpaying or omitting zakat on wealth that may be partially base metal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
According to the passage, dirhams are either silver-dominant (ghalib al-fiddah) or alloy-dominant (ghalib al-ghish). The first is treated like pure silver for zakat; the second is not.
The threshold for dominance is not specified here; later scholars often use 14 qirats per dirham.
2
Ruling for silver-dominant dirhams
If silver is the majority, the alloy is considered consumed (mustahlak) and the dirham is legally equivalent to pure silver. Zakat is wajib if the total silver weight reaches nisab (200 dirhams of pure silver or equivalent value).
Extraction of silver is not required; presence of nisab weight suffices according to the preferred view.
3
Ruling for alloy-dominant dirhams
If the alloy dominates, the dirham is not treated as silver. Instead, check if the dirhams are circulating as currency (ra'ijah) or owned for trade (nawa al-tijarah). If yes, assess their market value; if it reaches the nisab of the lowest pure silver dirhams, zakat is wajib on that value. If not circulating and not for trade, no zakat unless the actual silver content can be extracted and reaches 200 dirhams.
If extraction is impossible, the silver is considered perished and no zakat is due.
4
Consider the ghitārifa dirhams exception
Some dirhams like ghitārifa are treated differently: according to one view, zakat is due by count (5 dirhams per 200) because they are the dearest currency. The preferred view in the source is to treat them as trade goods and assess value.
This shows that local custom (urf) can influence the ruling.
5
Apply the preferred view on extraction
The passage notes a difference of opinion on whether extraction of pure silver from mixed dirhams is a condition for zakat. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that extraction is not required; it suffices that the dirhams contain silver reaching nisab in weight. This is supported by Ghayat al-Bayan.
This eases the obligation, as extraction may be impractical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وغالب الورق ورق لا عكسه يعني أن الدراهم إذا كانت مغشوشة، فإن كان الفضة الغالب هو فهي كالدراهم الخالصة لأن الغش فيها مستهلك لا فرق في مطلقاً الزيوف والنبهرجة، وما غلب فضته على غشه تناوله اسم الدراهم والشرع أوجب باسم الدراهم، فإن غلب الغش فليس كالفضة كالستوقة فينظر إن كانت رائجة أو نوى التجارة اعتبرت قيمتها فإن بلغت نصاباً من أدنى الدراهم التي تجب فيها الزكاة وهي التي غلبت فضتها وجبت فيها الزكاة وإلا فلا. وإن لم تكن أثماناً رائجة ولا منوية للتجارة فلا زكاة فيها إلا أن يكون ما فيها من الفضة يبلغ مائتي درهم بأن كانت كثيرة ويتخلص من الغش لأن الصفر لا تجب الزكاة فيها إلا بنية التجارة والفضة لا يشترط فيها نية التجارة، فإن كان ما فيها لا يتخلص فلا شيء عليه لأن الفضة فيه قد هلكت كذا في كثير من الكتب. وفي غاية البيان: الظاهر أن خلوص الفضة من الدراهم ليس بشرط، بل المعتبر أن تكون في الدراهم فضة بقدر النصاب فأما الغطارفة فقيل : يجب في كل مائتين منها خمسة منها عدداً أعز الأثمان والنقود عندهم. وقال السلف : ينظر إن كانت أثماناً رائجة أو سلعاً للتجارة تجب الزكاة في قيمتها كالفلوس وإن لم تكن للتجارة فلا زكاة فيها لأن ما فيها من الأخرى ليتم أربعة مثاقيل وأربعين درهماً لأن الزكاة لا تجب عنده في الكسور ا هـ . قوله : (وذكره في فتح القدير الخ) ظاهر كلام المؤلف الميل إليه، وفي السراج إلا أن الأول وهو أربعة عشر قيراطاً عليه الجم الغفير والجمهور الكثير وإطباق كتب المتقدمين والمتأخرين .
His saying: 'And the majority of silver is silver, not its opposite' means that if dirhams are mixed (maghshushah), then if the silver is the majority, they are like pure dirhams because the alloy is considered consumed (mustahlak). There is no difference generally between zayuf (counterfeit) and nabharaj (debased). What has silver dominant over its alloy is included in the name 'dirhams', and the Law obligated zakat by the name 'dirhams'. If the alloy dominates, it is not like silver, like a ustuqah (base metal). So look: if they are circulating (ra'ij) or intended for trade (nawa al-tijarah), their value is considered; if it reaches the nisab of the lowest dirhams in which zakat is obligated (i.e., those whose silver is dominant), then zakat is obligated on them, otherwise not. If they are not circulating and not intended for trade, then there is no zakat on them unless the silver in them reaches two hundred dirhams (i.e., if there are many, and the silver can be extracted). Because copper has no zakat except with trade intent, while silver does not require trade intent. If the silver cannot be extracted, then nothing is due because the silver has perished. This is mentioned in many books. In Ghayat al-Bayan: Apparently, extraction of silver from the dirhams is not a condition; rather consideration is that there is silver in the dirhams equal to the nisab. As for ghitārifa dirhams, it is said: zakat is obligatory on every two hundred of them, five dirhams in number, because they are the dearest of currencies among them. The predecessors said: look, if they are circulating or trade goods, zakat is obligated on their value like fulus (copper coins); if not for trade, no zakat, because the silver in them is less than four mithqals and forty dirhams? Actually, zakat is not obligated on fractions. End. His saying: 'And it is mentioned in Fath al-Qadir...' The apparent wording of the author inclines to it. In al-Siraj: except that the first (i.e., fourteen qirats) is upon the great multitude and the large majority and the agreement of the books of the early and later scholars.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expounds on the ruling for mixed (maghshushah) dirhams regarding zakat. The key principle: if the majority is silver (ghalib al-fiddah), the dirham is treated like pure silver because the alloy is considered legally consumed by the dominance of silver. If the majority is alloy (ghalib al-ghish), then the dirham does not have the legal status of silver. In that case, if the dirhams are circulating as currency (ra'ijah) or owned for trade, their market value is assessed. If that value reaches the nisab of the lowest pure silver dirhams, zakat is due. If they are not circulating and not for trade, then no zakat unless the actual silver content (if extractable) reaches 200 dirhams of pure silver. The text also mentions a difference of opinion on whether extraction is a condition; the preferred view is that it is not required. For ghitārifa dirhams, there are two views: either count them as standard dirhams for zakat, or treat them as commodities (like fulus) where zakat is based on value. The author inclines to the first view (counting them), and the text notes that the majority of scholars adopt the position of fourteen qirats (i.e., majority silver) as the threshold.

Key Terms
مغشوشة (maghshūshah): Dirhams mixed with alloy (copper) reducing silver purity.
الستوقة (al-ustūqah): Base metal items that are not used as currency; no zakat unless trade goods.
الغلبة (al-ghalabah): Dominance in quantity or value; here, the metal that constitutes the majority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zakat is due because they contain some silver.
B) No zakat unless the silver content can be extracted and reaches 200 dirhams.
C) Zakat is due on the value as trade goods.
D) They are treated like pure dirhams regardless.

Key Takeaway
For zakat on mixed dirhams, the legal status hinges on which metal dominates, the currency's circulation, and trade intent, with extraction not being a required condition in the relied-upon view.

Zakat on Trade Goods: Definition and Conditions
Definition of 'ard and ruling on zakat with trade intent condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'ard (trade good) and identify the conditions that make zakat on trade goods obligatory.

Core Idea
A trade good is any property except gold/silver, owned with trade intent at acquisition — without that intent, no zakat is due.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the precise meaning of 'urūḍ al-tijārah (trade goods) in Hanafi fiqh. The author carefully distinguishes the technical legal definition from linguistic usage, emphasizing that the crucial element is the intention to trade at the moment of acquisition. Understanding this definition is essential for correctly determining when zakat is due on movable property.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the legal definition of 'ard
Identify that in fiqh, 'ard (with sukun) means everything except gold and silver. This excludes cash and includes animals, but livestock already covered under sa'imah are excluded because zakat on them was mentioned separately.
The text cites al-Sihah: 'every commodity is 'ard except dirhams and dinars'.
2
Identify the condition of trade intent
The text states the ruling is restricted to goods 'that are for trade'. If goods are acquired for other purposes (e.g., personal use, rental, agriculture), no zakat is due on them as trade goods.
Goods for ghallah (produce) are not for sale.
3
Apply the condition to a borderline case
The author gives the example of a slave bought for service with an optional intention to sell if profit is found. The ruling: no zakat, because the primary intent at acquisition was service, not trade.
Later change of intent does not retroactively impose zakat.
4
Note the exclusion of land
The text mentions Mulla Khusraw's answer that land is not considered 'urūḍ based on Abu Ubayd's definition of trade goods as items not measured by weight or volume. Hence, even if land is bought with trade intent, it does not fall under 'urūḍ al-tijārah for zakat purposes.
This is a Hanafi specificity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: 'And on trade goods that reached the nisab of silver or gold' is connected to his saying at the beginning of the chapter: 'on two hundred dirhams' i.e., one-fourth of a tenth is obligatory on trade goods if they reach a nisab of either. [The word 'urūd] is the plural of 'ard, but with fathah on the ra' means 'the goods of this world' in al-Mughrib, but it is not appropriate here because it would include cash. So the correct is that it is the plural of 'ard with sukun, which as in Diya' al-Hulūm is 'what is not cash'. In al-Sihāh: al-'ard with sukun on the ra' means commodity, and everything is 'ard except dirhams and dinars. End. Thus it includes animals, and it is not objected that animals kept for milk and offspring are excluded because the intended meaning is other than those, since zakat on grazing livestock was already mentioned. Al-'urd (with dammah) means a side of something, hence 'he bequeathed a side of his property' i.e., a portion without specification. Al-'ard (with kasrah) means that for which a man is praised or blamed when it exists or not. Thus in Mi'raj al-Dirayah. It is restricted to being for trade because if it is for produce (ghallah), there is no zakat on it since it is not for sale. If someone buys a slave for service, intending to sell if profit is found, there is no zakat [on it]. And it is not objected that there is an obstacle to trade intent in the good, like buying kharaj land with trade intent, or buying 'ushr land and planting it, as they are not considered trade because of the contradiction mentioned. And the answer of Mulla Khusraw is that land is not from 'urūd based on Abu Ubayd's interpretation of it as what is not measured by weight or volume.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines 'urūd al-tijārah (trade goods) and their zakat ruling. The key point: 'ard (singular of 'urūd) is anything other than gold and silver that is owned for the purpose of trade. The author clarifies the correct linguistic and legal definition: 'ard with sukun on the ra' means any property except dirhams and dinars. This includes animals, but excludes livestock already covered under zakat on grazing animals (sa'imah). The essential condition for zakat on trade goods is the intention (niyyah) to trade when acquiring them. If goods are bought for personal use (like a slave for service), no zakat is due even if later sold. Similarly, land bought for cultivation cannot be trade goods because the use (ghallah) contradicts trade intent. The text ends with a reference to Mulla Khusraw's answer that land is not considered 'urūd according to Abu Ubayd's definition, so the issue of trade intent on land is moot.

Key Terms
عرض (جمع عروض) (‘arḍ (pl. ‘urūḍ)): Any property that is not gold or silver, intended for trade.
نية التجارة (niyyat al-tijārah): Intent to trade at the time of acquisition; if absent, the good is not trade property for zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Trade goods for zakat require both a non-monetary nature and a trade intention at the time of ownership.

Zakat on Mixed Silver and Gold Coins (Ghash)
Explaining zakat rulings for coins mixed with alloy and determining nisab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the zakat obligation on mixed-metal coins by applying the Hanafi principle of predominance (ghalaba) and the precautionary ruling for equal mixtures.

Core Idea
For zakat on mixed coins, the ruling follows the predominant metal; if equal, precaution obliges zakat.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rulings on zakat for coins that are not pure gold or silver but mixed with base metals like copper. The key concept is 'mustahlik' — when the precious metal overwhelms the alloy, the coin is legally treated as pure. A critical point arises when the proportions are equal, leading to a precautionary obligation according to later authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the composition scenario
First, determine whether the precious metal (silver/gold) is predominant (ghalib), equal to the alloy, or lesser. The ruling depends on this balance.
Predominance is based on weight, not value.
2
Apply the 'mustahlik' principle
If the precious metal predominates, the alloy is legally 'consumed' and ignored; the coin is treated as pure silver or gold for zakat purposes.
Thus, nisab is assessed on the total weight of the coin.
3
Handle the equal mixture ikhtilaf
When the alloy and precious metal are equal, there is a difference of opinion. The later Hanafi authorities (al-Khāniyah, al-Khulāṣah) chose obligation (wujūb) as a precautionary measure.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Distinguish gold-silver mixtures
If the mixture involves both gold and silver (e.g., a coin with both metals), each metal is considered separately: if one metal is predominant, the coin takes its ruling; if both are present with predominance, each is checked against its own nisab.
Gold is more valuable, so when it dominates, the whole is treated as gold.
5
Calculate zakat in a real example
Work through a practical example: given a mixed coin, identify the predominant metal, determine if its weight reaches nisab, and then apply the zakat rate.
Remember: nisab for silver is 595g, for gold 85g.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفضة مستهلك لغلبة النحاس عليها فكانت كالستوقة. وفي البدائع : وقول السلف أصح وحكم الذهب المغشوش كالفضة المغشوشة وقيد المصنف بالغالب لأن الغش والفضة لو استويا ففيه اختلاف واختار في الخانية والخلاصة الوجوب احتياطاً. وفي معراج الدراية : وكذا لا تباع إلا وزناً. وفي المجتبى المفهوم من كتاب الصرف أن للمساوي حكم الذهب والفضة، ومما ذكر في الزكاة أنه لا يكون له حكم الذهب والفضة وقيدنا المخالط للورق بأن يكون غشاً لأنه لو كان ذهباً فإن كانت الفضة مغلوبة فكله ذهب لأنه أعز وأغلى قيمة، وإن كانت الفضة غالبة فإن بلغ الذهب نصابه ففيه زكاة الذهب وإن بلغت الفضة نصابها فزكاة الفضة. وفي المغرب : الغطر يفية كانت من أعز النقود ببخارى منسوبة إلى غطريف عطاء بن الكندي أمير خراسان أيام الرشيد.
Silver that is consumed due to the predominance of copper over it, so it is like al-sutūqah (a type of alloy). And in al-Badā'i': The statement of the Salaf is more correct. And the ruling of debased gold is like debased silver. The author restricted it to the predominant because if the alloy and silver are equal, there is a difference of opinion, and in al-Khāniyah and al-Khulāṣah they chose obligation as a precaution. And in Mir'āj al-Dirāyah: Likewise, it should not be sold except by weight. And in al-Mujtabā, what is understood from the Book of Ṣarf is that the equal has the ruling of gold and silver, and what is mentioned in Zakat is that it does not have the ruling of gold and silver. And we restricted the mixture with silver to the specific alloy, because if it is gold, then if silver is dominated, it is all gold because it is more precious and valuable; if silver is dominant, then if the gold reaches its nisab, there is zakat of gold, and if the silver reaches its nisab, then zakat of silver. And in al-Maghrib: al-Ghaṭariyyah was from the most valuable currencies in Bukhara, attributed to Ghaṭrīf 'Aṭā' ibn al-Kindī, the governor of Khurasan during the time of al-Rashīd.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq discusses the zakat ruling on coins made of mixed metals (ghash). The primary issue is when silver (or gold) is mixed with a base metal like copper. The text establishes that if the precious metal is predominant (غالب), it is treated as pure silver or gold for zakat purposes, because the base metal is 'consumed' (مستهلك) by the predominant metal. If the two are equal, there is a difference: some later Hanafi authorities (like in al-Khāniyah and al-Khulāṣah) opt for obligation (الوجوب احتياطاً) as a precaution. The text then covers the case when the mixture is with gold rather than copper: if silver is dominant, then each metal is checked against its own nisab; if gold is dominant, it is all considered gold. This is a key ruling for merchants and those dealing in old coins.

Key Terms
الغش (al-ghash): The alloy mixed with gold or silver, usually a base metal like copper.
مستهلك (mustahlik): Consumed; refers to the base metal being so overwhelmed by the precious metal that it is legally ignored.
نصاب (niṣāb): The minimum threshold of wealth (85g gold or 595g silver) that triggers zakat obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the term 'mustahlik' mean in the context of mixed coins?
A) The alloy is consumed by the precious metal and legally ignored.
B) The coin is broken and unusable.
C) The precious metal is wasted.
D) The nisab is reduced.

Key Takeaway
When assessing zakat on mixed coins, first determine if the precious metal predominates; if equal, the precautionary position obliges zakat on the entire coin.

Zakat on Mixed-Gold Coins: Predominant vs. Non-Predominant
Explaining zakat on gold coins with varying purity, distinguishing between currency and trade goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on zakat for gold coins of varying purity by determining whether the coin is predominantly gold or not, and whether it is used as currency or for trade.

Core Idea
Zakat on mixed coins depends on purity and usage: gold-dominant coins treated as pure; others valued as currency or weighed separately.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that expands on the ruling for gold coins that are not pure. The author distinguishes between coins where gold is predominant (treated as pure gold) and those where it is not. For non-predominant coins, their use matters: if used as currency or trade goods, zakat is on the total value; otherwise, the gold and silver weights are assessed separately. This nuance is crucial for merchants dealing in various coin types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the coin type
Determine if the coin is predominantly gold (like ṣūriyyah) or not (like harawiyyah/marawiyyah).
Predominance means more than half the weight is gold.
2
Apply the rule for gold-dominant coins
If the coin is predominantly gold, it is treated exactly like pure gold for zakat purposes.
3
Check the coin's function
For non-predominant coins, ask: is it used as circulating currency (thaman rā'ij) or held for trade (tijārah)?
If yes, proceed to step 4a; if no, step 4b.
4
Determine the zakat base
If the coin is used as currency or for trade, zakat is calculated on its total market value. Otherwise, weigh the gold and silver content separately (each is purified by melting) and check if either reaches nisab.
The passage implies zakat is due on each metal that reaches nisab.
5
Reflect on the restriction
Note that the original ruling being commented on applied only when coins are not currency or trade goods; this passage clarifies the exception.
This is an expansion (tafsīl) of a general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وقيدنا المخالط للورق الخ في البدائع : وكذا حكم الدنانير التي الغالب عليها الذهب والصورية ونحوهما فحكمها وحكم الذهب الخالص سواء، وأما الهروية والمروية مما لم يكن الغالب فيها الذهب فتعتبر قيمتها إن كانت ثمناً رائجاً أو للتجارة وإلا فيعتبر قدر ما فيها من الذهب والفضة وزناً لأن كل واحد منهما يخلص بالإذابة ا فتأمله ما هنا فإنه يفيد تقييد ما هنا فإنه يفيد تقييد ما هنا بما إذا لم يكن ثمناً رائجاً ولا للتجارة .
His saying (And we restricted the mixture with silver, etc.) In al-Badā'i': Likewise the ruling of dinars that are predominantly gold, and the ṣūriyyah and the like, their ruling and the ruling of pure gold are the same. As for the harawiyyah and marawiyyah, which are not predominantly gold, their value is considered if they are circulating currency or for trade; otherwise, the quantity of gold and silver in them is considered by weight, because each one can be purified by melting. So reflect on this, for it indicates a restriction here to what is not circulating currency nor for trade.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage, commenting on the author's phrase 'wa qayyadnā al-mukhāliṭ lil-waraq', provides further details from al-Badā'i' on gold coins of varying purity. Coins that are predominantly gold (like ṣūriyyah) are treated as pure gold. However, coins that are not predominantly gold (like harawiyyah and marawiyyah) are treated differently: if they serve as currency (thaman rā'ij) or are trade goods, their entire value (qīmah) is considered for zakat; otherwise, one must assess the weight of gold and silver separately because they can be purified by melting. This nuance is important for merchants dealing in various coin types.

Key Terms
صورية (Ṣūriyyah): A type of gold coin that is predominantly gold, treated like pure gold for zakat.
هروية (Harawiyyah): A type of gold coin that is not predominantly gold; contains significant silver alloy.
مروية (Marawiyyah): Similar to harawiyyah, a low-purity gold coin.
ثمن رائج (Thaman rā'ij): Circulating currency – a medium of exchange commonly used in transactions.
تخلّص بالإذابة (Takhallus bi-l-idhābah): Purification by melting – the process of separating gold and silver by melting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Zakat is due on the weight of gold and silver separately.
C) No zakat is due because it is not pure gold.
D) Zakat is due on the gold content only, ignoring silver.

Key Takeaway
For zakat on mixed gold coins, check purity percentage and the coin's function (currency/trade or not) to determine the correct basis of calculation.

Definition of Trade Goods and Condition of Trade Intent
Defining trade goods linguistically and legally, and explaining the condition of trade intent for zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'urūḍ in Hanafi fiqh and explain the condition of trade intent (niyyat al-tijārah) for zakat, including how stronger actions override trade intent.

Core Idea
Trade goods are everything except gold and silver; trade intent is essential but can be nullified by a stronger action like planting.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq, the commentator clarifies the correct definition of ‘urūḍ (trade goods) as all property other than gold and silver coins. He then elaborates on the essential condition that such goods must be intended for trade (tijārah) to be subject to zakat. A key subtlety is that a stronger practical act—like planting seeds—can override the trade intent, shifting the obligation from zakat to agricultural 'ushr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the correct linguistic definition of ‘urūḍ
Distinguish between the plural forms ‘araḍ (with fatḥa) and ‘arḍ (with sukūn). The commentator argues that the correct term here is ‘arḍ (sukūn) meaning everything except naqd (gold/silver coins).
Refer to dictionaries: al-Ṣiḥāḥ, Ḍiyā’ al-Ḥulūm.
2
Determine the scope of ‘urūḍ
Animals are included because they are not naqd, but livestock already subject to zakat (sā’imah) are excluded due to prior mention.
Avoid confusion with grazing livestock.
3
Apply the condition of trade intent
The goods must be intended for trade (tijārah) at the time of acquisition. If intended for personal use or produce (ghallah), no zakat.
Example: slave bought for service with contingent sale intent still not trade.
4
Recognize how a stronger action overrides trade intent
Planting seeds intended for trade negates trade status and invokes ‘ushr instead. This is because the act of planting is a stronger indication of non-trade.
Compare with buying land and planting: same principle.
5
Address apparent objections
The commentator refutes Mulla Khusraw’s view that land is not ‘urūḍ, and drops al-Zayla‘ī’s objection regarding seeds.
The correct Hanafi position is reaffirmed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وفي عروض تجارة بلغت نصاب ورق أو ذهب معطوف على قوله أول الباب في مائتي درهم» أي يجب ربع العشر في عروض التجارة إذا بلغت نصاباً أحدهما من جمع عرض لكنه بفتح الراء حطام الدنيا في المغرب لكنه ليس بمناسب هنا لأنه يدخل فيه النقدان، فالصواب أن يكون جمع عرض بسكونها وهو كما في ضياء الحلوم ما ليس بنقد. وفي الصحاح : العرض بسكون الراء المتاع وكل شيء فهو عرض سوى الدراهم والدنانير اهـ. فيدخل الحيوان ولا يرد عليه ما أسيم من الحيوانات للدر والنسل لظهور أن المراد غيره لتقدم ذكر زكاة السوائم. والعُرض بالضم الجانب منه ومنه أوصى بعرض ما له أي جانب منه بلا تعيين والعرض بكسر العين ما يحمد الرجل ويذم عند وجوده وعدمه. كذا معراج الدراية . قيد بكونها للتجارة لأنها لو كانت للغلة فلا زكاة فيها لأنها ليست للمبايعة . ولو اشترى عبداً للخدمة ناوياً بيعه إن وجد ربحاً لا زكاة فيه ولا يرد عليه ما إذا كان في العرض مانع من نية التجارة كأن اشترى أرض خراج ناوياً للتجارة أو اشترى أرض عشر وزرعها فإنها لا تكون للتجارة لما يلزم عليه من الثني كما قدمناه وجواب منلاخسرو بأن الأرض ليست من العروض بناء على تفسير أبي عبيد إياها بما لا يدخله كيل ولا وزن ولا يكون عقاراً ولا حيواناً ،مردود لما علمت أن الصواب تفسيرها هنا بما ليس بنقد ولذا لا يرد على المصنف ما لو اشترى بذرا للتجارة وزرعه فإنه لا زكاة فيه وإنما يجب العشر فيه لأن بذره في الأرض أبطل كونه للتجارة لأن مجرد كونه نوى الخدمة في عبد التجارة أسقط وجوب الزكاة فلأن يسقط التصرف الأقوى أولى . وكذا لو لم يزرعه ففيه الزكاة وبهذا سقط اعتبار الزيلعي كما لا يخفى .
His saying (And on trade goods that reach the nisab of silver or gold) is connected to his saying at the beginning of the chapter 'on two hundred dirhams' i.e., one-fourth of a tenth is obligatory on trade goods when they reach the nisab of either of them.” “Urūḍ” is the plural of ‘araḍ but with fatḥa on the rā, meaning worldly goods in al-Maghrib, but it is not appropriate here because it includes cash. So the correct form is the plural of ‘arḍ with sukūn, which is, as in Ḍiyā’ al-Ḥulūm, what is not cash. And in al-Ṣiḥāḥ: ‘arḍ with sukūn is merchandise; everything is ‘arḍ except dirhams and dinars. So it includes animals, and it is not refuted by animals that are pastured for milk and offspring, because it is clear that other animals are intended, since zakat on grazing livestock has already been mentioned. And al-‘urḍ with ḍamma is the side of something; from it is ‘he bequeathed the ‘urḍ of his property’ i.e., a portion of it without specification. And al-‘irḍ with kasr is what a person is praised or blamed for in its presence or absence. So from Mir’āj al-Dirāyah. He restricted it to being for trade because if it is for produce, there is no zakat on it because it is not for sale. And if one buys a slave for service with the intention to sell if a profit is found, there is no zakat on it. And it is not refuted by what if there is an obstacle to the intention of trade in the good, such as if one buys kharaj land with intention of trade, or buys 'ushr land and plants it, then it does not become for trade because of the secondary liability as we have mentioned. And the answer of Mulla Khusraw that land is not from ‘urūḍ based on Abū ‘Ubayd’s interpretation of it as what is not measured by weight or volume, nor is it real estate or animals, is refuted, because you know that the correct interpretation here is what is not cash. Therefore, it does not refute the author's example of buying seeds for trade and planting them, for there is no zakat on it, rather 'ushr is obligatory on it, because his planting the seeds negates its being for trade, because merely intending service on a trade slave dropped the obligation of zakat, so a stronger action should drop it even more. Likewise, if he does not plant it, there is zakat on it, and by this the objection of al-Zayla'ī is dropped, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn phrase 'wa fī ‘urūḍ tijārah balaghat niṣāb waraq aw dhahab'. The commentator first clarifies the correct linguistic definition of ‘urūḍ (trade goods) as everything other than gold and silver coins (naqd). He cites dictionaries and shows that animals are included, but livestock already pastured for produce are excluded because their zakat was previously discussed. He then emphasizes the condition that the goods must be intended for trade (tijārah). If they are for produce (ghallah)—like land for crops—then no zakat is due because they are not for sale. A key point: if someone buys a slave for service with a vague hope to sell if profit arises, no zakat is due because the primary intent is not trade. The text then addresses objections from al-Zayla'ī and Mulla Khusraw regarding land and seeds. The crux is that a stronger action (like planting seeds) overrides the weaker intention of trade, so seeds planted for trade lose trade status and become subject to 'ushr instead. This is a classic Hanafi principle of 'stronger action prevailing'.

Key Terms
العروض (al-‘urūḍ): Trade goods; all property other than gold and silver coins (naqd).
نية التجارة (niyyat al-tijārah): The intention to trade the goods for profit, distinct from personal use or produce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All movable property.
B) Everything except gold and silver coins (naqd).
C) Merchandise measured by weight or volume.
D) Livestock only.

Key Takeaway
Trade goods ('urūḍ) are all property except gold and silver, and must be intended for trade to be subject to zakat; a stronger action like planting overrides trade intent.

Trade Intent Remains in Substitutes (Badal)
Explaining that trade intention persists in the substitute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that trade intent persists in substitutes (badal) and apply it to scenarios involving exchange, compensation, and rental income.

Core Idea
The substitute inherits the trade intent automatically — no new intention needed.

When a merchant exchanges trade goods for cash or receives compensation for a lost trade asset, does the newly acquired property automatically become a trade good? The Hanafi school rules that the intent for trade (niyyat al-tijarah) transfers to the substitute (badal) by operation of law, without requiring a fresh intention. This short passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the principle and its exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The core ruling: the substitute (badal) acquires the trade status of the original. Understand that no new intention is required.
This is the rule: hukm al-badal hukm al-asl.
2
Understand the reasoning
The maxim 'hukm al-badal hukm al-asl' (the ruling of the substitute is the ruling of the original) underlies this automatic transfer.
The substitute stands in place of the original for legal purposes.
3
Examine the exception
If compensation arises from intentional wrongdoing (e.g., intentional killing), the substitute does not become trade property because it is a penalty, not a genuine replacement.
Exception: 'amd (intentional) vs. khata' (error).
4
Extend to other cases
Rental income from a trade property (e.g., a trade house or trade slave) is treated like the price of trade goods, so it also inherits trade intent.
Ujrat dar al-tijarah wa 'abd al-tijarah are like thaman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن نية التجارة في الأصل تعتبر ثابتة في بدله وإن لم يتحقق شخصها فيه وهو ما قوبض به مال التجارة فإنه يكون للتجارة بلا نية لأن حكم البدل حكم الأصل، وكذا لو كان العبد للتجارة فقتله عبد خطأ ودفع به فإن المدفوع يكون للتجارة بخلاف القتل عمداً، وأجرة دار التجارة وعبد التجارة بمنزلة ثمن مال التجارة في الصحيح من الرواية. كذا في الخانية .
And know that the intention for trade in the original is considered established in its substitute, even if its specific identity is not realized in it; that which is received in exchange for trade property becomes for trade without an intention, because the ruling of the substitute is the ruling of the original. Similarly, if a slave was for trade and he was killed by a slave in error, and he (the killer) is handed over in compensation, then the handed-over one becomes for trade, unlike intentional killing. And the rental income of a trade house and a trade slave are like the price of trade property in the sound narration. Thus in al-Khāniyah.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage establishes a key Hanafi principle: when trade goods are exchanged (e.g., sold), the property received in exchange (thaman, badal) automatically takes on the status of trade goods, even without a new intention. This applies to any substitute: if a trade slave is killed accidentally and the owner receives a compensatory slave (diya), that slave becomes a trade good. However, if the killing is intentional, the substitute does not become trade property (because the compensation is not a direct replacement of the trade asset, but rather a penalty). Similarly, rentals from a trade property are treated like the price of trade goods. This ensures continuity of trade status across transactions.

Key Terms
البدل (al-badal): The substitute property received in exchange for trade goods, such as the price or compensation.
أجرة دار التجارة (ujrat dār al-tijārah): Rental income from a property that is a trade good; it is treated like the price of trade goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It requires a new intention to become a trade good.
B) It automatically becomes a trade good without an intention.
C) It does not become a trade good because it was received as compensation.
D) It becomes a trade good only if the owner intends to sell it.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the intent for trade persists in the substitute automatically, ensuring continuity of trade status for zakat purposes, with the exception of compensation for intentional wrongdoing.

Mudārib vs. Rabb al-Māl: Trade Goods Acquired without Intent
Distinguishing zakat liability between mudārib and rabb al-māl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a mudārib's purchases are automatically trade goods to determine zakat liability, and distinguish this from a rabb al-māl's purchases where intent matters.

Core Idea
A mudārib's every purchase is for trade; zakat is due even without trade intent.

In a mudārabah partnership, the working partner (mudārib) is contractually bound to trade with the capital. This restriction has a direct impact on zakat: any item he acquires, even if seemingly for personal use, is treated as a trade good and subject to zakat. In contrast, the capital owner (rabb al-māl) can acquire goods for personal use, and zakat only applies if he intends to sell them. This passage from al-Kāfī illustrates the distinction and its applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties
Recognize the mudārib (working partner) and the rabb al-māl (capital owner) in a mudārabah partnership.
2
State the ruling for each
For the mudārib: all acquisitions are trade goods, so zakat is due. For the rabb al-māl: only acquisitions intended for trade are trade goods.
3
Understand the reasoning
The mudārib cannot purchase for non-trade due to the partnership contract; the rabb al-māl can purchase for personal use.
The mudārib's purchase power is restricted to trade only.
4
Apply to the examples in the passage
The mudārib buys a slave, garment, food, and mount—all are trade goods. The rabb al-māl buys a garment and mount—not trade goods unless intended for sale.
Notice the difference in treatment despite the same items.
5
Note the nuance with accessories
Items like saddles and bottles are trade goods if given with the main sale; a garment of a slave is a mere follower and not a trade good.
This shows the importance of whether an item is intended as part of the trade.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر في الكافي : ولو ابتاع مضارب عبداً وثوباً له وطعاماً وحمولة وجبت الزكاة في الكل وإن قصد غير التجارة لأنه لا يملك الشراء إلا للتجارة بخلاف رب المال حيث لا يزكي الثوب والحمولة لأنه يملك الشراء لغير التجارة .اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : ويحمل عدم تزكية الثوب لرب المال ما دام لم يقصد بيعه معه فإنه ذكر في فتاوى قاضيخان : النخاس إذا باع دواب للبيع واشترى لها جلالاً ومقاود، فإن كان لا ذلك يدفع . مع الدابة إلى المشتري لا زكاة فيها، وإن كان يدفعها معها وجب فيها وكذا العطار إذا اشترى قوارير اهـ. وقد يفرق بأن ثوب العبد يدخل في بيعه بلا ذكر تبعاً حتى لا يكون له قسط من الثمن فلم يكن مقصوداً أصلاً فوجوده كعدمه بخلاف جل الدواب والقوارير فإنه قصداً، ولذا لم يدخل في المبيع بلا ذكر.
And it is mentioned in al-Kāfī: If a mudārib buys a slave, a garment for him, food, and a mount, zakat is due on all, even if he intended non-trade, because he cannot purchase except for trade, unlike the rabb al-māl, who does not give zakat on the garment and the mount because he can purchase for non-trade. In Fatḥ al-Qadīr: The absence of zakat on the garment for the rabb al-māl is understood as long as he did not intend to sell it with him, for it is mentioned in Fatāwā Qāḍī Khān: The horse dealer who sells horses and buys for them saddles and halters: if he does not give them with the horse to the buyer, there is no zakat on them; if he gives them with it, zakat is obligatory. Similarly, the perfume seller if he buys bottles. And one may distinguish by saying that the garment of the slave enters into his sale without mention as a follower, so it has no share of the price, so it was not intended at all, thus its existence is like non-existence, unlike the saddles of animals and the bottles, which are intended, and therefore they do not enter into the sale without mention.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage from al-Kāfī addresses a crucial distinction between a mudārib (agent in a mudārabah partnership) and the rabb al-māl (capital owner). The mudārib is restricted by the partnership contract to trade only—he cannot purchase goods for personal use. Therefore, any item he acquires, even if seemingly for personal use like a garment, is considered a trade good and subject to zakat. In contrast, the rabb al-māl (or an ordinary trader) can purchase items for personal use without trade intent, so zakat is not due on such items unless he intends to sell them. The passage then cites Fatāwā Qāḍī Khān: a horse dealer who buys accessories (saddles, halters) only owes zakat on them if they are given to the buyer with the horse (i.e., included in the sale). Otherwise, they are not trade goods. Finally, a distinction is made: the garment of a slave is a mere accessory that passes without mention in the sale, so it is not truly intended as trade; but saddles and bottles are more substantial and are intended for sale, so they are trade goods.

Key Terms
المضارب (al-muḍārib): The working partner in a mudārabah partnership; he can only purchase for trade.
رب المال (rabb al-māl): The capital owner in a mudārabah; he can purchase for personal use.
التجارة (al-tijārah): Trade; the activity of buying and selling with profit motive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A mudārib buys a horse and a saddle for personal use. According to the passage, what is the zakat ruling?
A) No zakat because he intended personal use.
B) Zakat is due on both because he cannot buy for non-trade.
C) Zakat on the horse only, not the saddle.
D) Zakat is due only if he sells them later.

Key Takeaway
A mudārib's purchases are always trade goods for zakat; a rabb al-māl's purchases are trade goods only if intended for trade.

Valuation of Trade Goods: The Ikhtilaf Between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
Explains the choice of currency for valuing trade goods and the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on valuing trade goods for zakat nisab when both currencies yield nisab, and apply the agreed principle when only one reaches nisab.

Core Idea
When only one valuation reaches nisab, agree on that; when both, Abu Hanifa picks obligation, Abu Yusuf picks purchase currency.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq, the author discusses the critical question of how to value trade goods for zakat when two currencies (silver and gold) are involved. While there is agreement on the basic rule when only one currency reaches nisab, a notable intra-Hanafi dispute arises when both valuations would satisfy the nisab threshold. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply zakat rules in complex commercial scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed rule
When valuation in only one of the two currencies reaches the nisab threshold, that currency must be used by agreement of all Hanafi imams. This is the default rule for avoiding doubt in obligation.
The text cites al-Nihāyah and al-Khulāṣah for this consensus.
2
Recognize the point of dispute
The ikhtilaf occurs when the trade goods’ value in both silver and gold would each independently reach their respective nisabs. The question becomes: which currency should be used?
The example from al-Ẓahīriyyah: a slave worth 200 dirhams (silver nisab) and 100 dinars (gold nisab).
3
Understand Abu Hanifa’s position
Abu Hanifa holds that the owner must value by the currency that makes zakat obligatory (in this case, gold) to ensure the poor’s right is fulfilled (دفعاً لحاجة الفقير).
This is a rights-based reasoning prioritizing the right of the poor.
4
Understand Abu Yusuf’s position
Abu Yusuf holds that the owner values by the currency used to purchase the goods (يقوم بما اشترى), regardless of which valuation triggers obligation.
This is a transactional approach linking valuation to the original medium of exchange.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical result) appears when the two valuations give different obligations. For example, if a slave was bought for 100 dinars and is now worth 200 dirhams, Abu Hanifa would assess zakat on 100 dinars (since gold nisab reached), while Abu Yusuf would assess on 200 dirhams (since silver nisab reached), but in most cases both may still result in zakat. The dispute matters when only one valuation triggers obligation.
See the madhhab_dispute_map for details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما قال نصاب ورق ولم يقل نصاب فضة لأن الورق - بكسر الراء - اسم للمضروب من الفضة كما في المغرب ولا بد أن تبلغ العروض قيمة نصاب من المضروبة كما في الذخيرة ،والخانية لأن لزومها مبني على التقوم والعرف أن تقوم بالمصكوك كما قدمناه. وأشار بقوله ورق أو ذهب إلى أنه مخير إن شاء قومها بالفضة وإن شاء بالذهب لأن الثمنين في تقدير قيم الأشياء بهما سواء. وفي النهاية : لو كان تقويمه بأحد النقدين يتم النصاب وبالآخر لا فإنه يقومه بما يتم به النصاب بالاتفاق .اهـ. وفي الخلاصة أيضاً ما يفيد الاتفاق على هذا وكل منهما ممنوع فقد قال في الظهيرية رجل له عبد للتجارة إن قوم بالدراهم لا تجب فيه الزكاة، وإن قوم بالدنانير تجب، فعند أبي حنيفة يقوم بما تجب فيه الزكاة دفعاً لحاجة الفقير وسداً لخلتة. وقال أبو يوسف : يقوم بما اشترى فإن اشتراه بغير النقدين
He only said 'nisab of waraq' and did not say 'nisab of silver' because waraq – with kasr on the rā – is the name for coined silver, as in al-Maghrib. And it is necessary that the trade goods reach the value of a nisab of coined metal, as in al-Dhakhīrah and al-Khāniyah, because its obligation is based on valuation (taqwīm) and the custom is to value with coined coins as we have presented. And he indicated by his saying 'waraq or gold' that he has a choice: if he wishes, he values them with silver, and if he wishes, with gold, because the two prices are equal in estimating the value of things. And in al-Nihāyah: If his valuation with one currency completes the nisab but with the other does not, then he values by that which completes the nisab, by agreement. In al-Khulāṣah there is also something indicating agreement on this, but both are prevented. For it is said in al-Ẓahīriyyah: A man has a slave for trade: if he values it with dirhams, zakat is not due; if he values it with dinars, zakat is due. According to Abu Hanifa, he values by that which makes zakat due, to fulfill the need of the poor and to close their deficiency. And Abu Yusuf said: He values by what he bought with; if he bought it with other than the two currencies...
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage addresses how to value trade goods for zakat nisab. The author explains why the matn says 'nisab of waraq' (silver coins) rather than 'nisab of silver' – because waraq refers specifically to coined silver, which is the customary standard for valuation. The general principle is that the owner may choose to value by silver or gold, and if only one achieves nisab, he must use that one (by agreement). However, there is a dispute when both valuations would yield nisab. The text relates a case from al-Ẓahīriyyah: if a trade slave is valued at less than silver nisab but more than gold nisab, then according to Abu Hanifa, the owner must use the gold valuation (the one that results in obligation) to assist the poor. Abu Yusuf, on the other hand, says the owner uses the currency with which he originally purchased the slave. The passage is cut off, but the ikhtilaf is clear.

Key Terms
الورق (al-waraq): Coined silver dirhams; specifically the minted currency.
التقوم (al-taqwīm): Valuation; assessing the worth of trade goods in terms of silver or gold coins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) By dirhams because silver is the default.
B) By dinars because that triggers zakat obligation.
C) By the currency used to purchase the slave.
D) The owner may choose.

Key Takeaway
When both currencies reach nisab, Abu Hanifa prioritizes the poor's right (value by obligation), while Abu Yusuf ties valuation to the original purchase currency.

Deficiency of Nisab During the Zakat Year Does Not Harm
Expansion of a matn ruling with rationale, disputes, and branches

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nisab deficiency during the zakat year, explain the rationale, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding value decrease and the effect of description change.

Core Idea
Nisab deficiency during the year is forgiven; only the beginning and end matter for zakat obligation.

The commentator expands on the matn's rule that nisab deficiency during the year does not harm. The law prioritizes practical ease by requiring nisab only at the start (to begin the year) and at the end (for obligation). This lesson unpacks the rationale, the crucial distinction between quantity and description changes, an intra-Hanafi dispute on value decrease, the effect of debt, and two illustrative branches.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its rationale
State the ruling from the matn: 'Deficiency of the nisab during the year does not harm if it is complete at both ends.' The rationale is that maintaining completeness throughout the year is impractical.
The beginning is needed to establish sufficiency (ghina) and commence the year; the end is needed because obligation arises at that moment.
2
Distinguish deficiency in amount vs. description
The ruling applies only to deficiency in the quantity (qadr) of nisab. If the legal description (wasf) changes — e.g., grazing livestock becomes fodder-fed — it is treated as total loss and breaks the year.
Change of description eliminates the zakatable category entirely, unlike a mere reduction in count or value.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on value decrease at year-end
Abu Hanifa holds that if the nisab decreases in value at the end of the year, no zakat is lost; the Sahibayn say zakat is due on the remaining value. The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's.
Thamarah al-khilaf: If 200 dirhams drop to 150, Abu Hanifa requires zakat on 200 (5 dirhams); Sahibayn require zakat on 150 (3.75 dirhams).
4
Know the majority view on debt during the year
Debt incurred during the year does not break the zakat year, even if it engulfs the nisab. Zufar dissents, but the relied-upon view is that the year continues.
This is recorded in al-Mujtaba and is the operative fatwa.
5
Apply the ruling to the branches (sheep and grape juice)
Two cases: (1) Sheep for trade die, but the hides retain value and reach nisab at year-end → zakat is due because the property remained intact (wool/hide valuable). (2) Grape juice becomes wine (valueless) then turns to vinegar before year-end → if it regains value, zakat is due per one narration because it reverted to trade.
The key is whether the property ever lost all zakatable value and whether it returned to its original purpose by year-end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (ونقصان النصاب في الحول لا يضران كمل في طرفيه لأنه يشق اعتبار الكمال في أثنائه أما لا بد منه في ابتدائه للانعقاد وتحقيق الغناء وفي انتهائه للوجوب ولا كذلك فيما ذلك لأنه حالة البقاء. قيد بنقصان النصاب أي قدره لأن زوال وصفه كهلاك الكل كما إذا جعل السائمة علوفة لأن العلوفة ليست من مال الزكاة أما بعد فوات بعض النصاب بقي بعض المحل صالحاً لبقاء الحول وشرط الكمال في الطرفين لنقصانه في الحول لأن نقصانه بعد الحول من حيث القيمة لا يسقط شيئاً من الزكاة عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما عليه زكاة ما بقي . كذا في الخلاصة. وذكر في المجتبى الدين في خلال الحول لا يقطع حكم الحول وإن كان مستغرقاً. وقال زفر : يقطع .اهـ. ومن فروع المسألة إذا كان له غنم للتجارة تساوي نصاباً فماتت قبل الحول فسلخها ودبغ جلدها فتم الحول كان عليه فيها الزكاة إن بلغت نصاباً، ولو كان له عصير للتجارة فتخمر قبل الحول ثم صار خلا فتم الحول لا زكاة فيها قالوا : لأن في الأول الصوف الذي على الجلد متقوم فيبقى الحول ببقائه، وفي الثاني بطل تقوم الكل بالخمرية فهلك كل المال إلا أنه يخالف ما روى ابن سماعة عن محمد : اشترى عصيراً قميته مائتا درهم فتخمر بعد أربعة أشهر، فلما مضى سبعة أشهر أو ثمانية أشهر إلا يوماً صار خلاً يساوي مائتي درهم فتمت السنة كان عليه الزكاة لأنه عاد للتجارة كما كان. كذا في الخانية.
His saying: (And deficiency of the nisab during the year does not harm if it is complete at both ends because it is difficult to consider completeness in its midst; rather, it is necessary at its beginning for commencement and realization of sufficiency, and at its end for obligation, and not so in the middle because that is a state of continuation. He restricted to deficiency of the nisab, meaning its amount, because loss of its description is like total loss, as when grazing livestock is made fodder-fed because fodder-fed is not from zakatable wealth. But after some of the nisab is lost, some of the basis remains suitable for the continuation of the year, and the condition of completeness at both ends is due to deficiency during the year, because its deficiency after the year in terms of value does not cancel any zakat according to Abu Hanifa, while according to the two disciples, he owes zakat on what remains. Thus in al-Khulasa. And it is mentioned in al-Mujtaba: debt during the year does not break the year's ruling even if it is overwhelming. And Zufar said: it breaks. End. And from the branches of the issue: if one has sheep for trade worth a nisab, and they die before the year, then he skins them and tans their hides, and the year completes, he owes zakat on them if they reach a nisab. And if one has grape juice for trade, which becomes wine before the year, then becomes vinegar, and the year completes, no zakat is due on it. They said: because in the first, the wool on the hide is valuable, so the year continues with its remaining value; in the second, the value of everything is nullified by being wine, so all wealth is lost. Except that it contradicts what Ibn Sama'ah narrated from Muhammad: one bought grape juice worth two hundred dirhams, it became wine after four months, then after seven or eight months less a day it became vinegar worth two hundred dirhams, and the year completed; zakat was due on it because it returned to trade as it was. Thus in al-Khaniyah.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn phrase: 'And deficiency of the nisab during the year does not harm if it is complete at both ends.' The commentator begins by explaining the wisdom behind this ruling: it is impractical to require the nisab to remain intact throughout the entire year, so the law requires completeness only at the beginning (when the year starts counting) and at the end (when zakat becomes due). The condition of the beginning is to establish the sufficiency of the owner and to initiate the year; the condition at the end is because obligation arises only at that moment. The middle period is one of 'remaining' (baqa'), not obligation, so fluctuations are overlooked. The commentator then clarifies that this ruling applies to deficiency in the actual amount (qadr) of the nisab, not a change in its legal description (wasf). For example, if grazing livestock (sa'imah) becomes fodder-fed ('ulufah), this changes the category entirely—fodder-fed animals are not normally zakatable—so this 'deficiency in description' effectively destroys the nisab like total loss. However, if only part of the nisab is lost, the remaining part still supports the year's continuation. A key intra-Hanafi dispute follows: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that if the nisab decreases in value at the end of the year (but remains in quantity), no zakat is lost; the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say zakat is due on whatever remains of value. This is recorded in al-Khulasa. Another issue: debt in the middle of the year does not interrupt the zakat year according to the majority (as in al-Mujtaba), but Zufar disagrees, saying it breaks the year. Then two illustrative cases are given: (1) sheep for trade dying before the year—if the skin after tanning retains value and reaches nisab, zakat is due; (2) grape juice for trade turning into wine then vinegar—if it becomes vinegar before year-end, no zakat because the wealth was lost when it became wine (haram and valueless for zakat). However, there is a counter case from Ibn Sama'ah: if the juice turned to wine mid-year but then became vinegar before the year ended, returning to its original value, zakat is due because the property reverted to the purpose of trade.

Key Terms
نقصان النصاب (nuqsan al-nisab): A decrease in the quantity or value of the nisab during the zakat year.
علوفة (‘ulufah): Fodder-fed livestock, as opposed to grazing livestock (sa'imah). The former is not subject to zakat in the same way.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of a debt that completely engulfs the nisab during the zakat year?
A) The year is broken and must restart after the debt is cleared.
B) The year is not broken; zakat is due on the full nisab at the end.
C) The year is not broken, but zakat is only due on the amount remaining after debt.
D) The year is broken only if the debt is incurred at the beginning of the year.

Key Takeaway
Nisab deficiency during the year does not break the zakat year as long as it is complete at both ends; changes in description or value at year-end can have different effects according to the Imams.

Combining Gold and Silver for Zakat: Value vs. Weight (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on combining gold and silver by value vs. weight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for combining gold and silver for zakat, explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the mufta bihi position to calculate zakat on mixed assets.

Core Idea
Gold and silver combine by value, not weight, because their monetary function creates legal homogeneity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the matn's ruling that gold and silver are added by value for zakat. While the matn states the rule, the commentary unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa (d. 150 AH) holds that the two currencies are combined by value, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, maintain combination by weight. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for correctly calculating zakat on assets that include both gold and silver, as well as on trade goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central rule
The matn states: gold and silver are added by value. Note that this applies when each category does not individually reach the nisab.
2
Understand the reasoning for adding trade goods
Trade goods are added to gold/silver because their zakatability depends on trade intention; the owner can intend trade for any purchase. This sets the context for the second part.
3
Analyze the homogeneity argument
Abu Hanifa argues that gold and silver share the same genus (jins) as currencies (thamanayn), and homogeneity is realized through value, not physical form. Hence, value-based addition. The Sahibayn argue that nisab is fixed by weight, so weight should be the measure.
4
Contrast the practical consequences
Apply the dispute to examples: a person with 105 dirhams and gold worth 100 dirhams. Abu Hanifa: zakat due (combined value 205). Sahibayn: no zakat (neither reaches nisab individually). Also, on surpluses: Sahibayn zakat on fractions proportionally; Abu Hanifa only if surplus reaches a full nisab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

القيمة دون الصورة فيضم بها. وفي المحيط : لو كان له مائة درهم وعشرة دنانير قيمتها أقل من مائة تجب الزكاة عندهما. واختلفوا على قوله والصحيح الوجوب لأنه إن لم يمكن تكميل نصاب الدراهم باعتبار قيمة باعتبار قيمة الذهب بالفضة يتم باعتبار قيمة الفضة بالذهب، فكيف يكون تعليلاً لعدم اعتبار القيمة مطلقاً عند تكامل الأجزاء مع يرد عليه لو زادت قيمة أحدهما ولم تنقص قيمة الآخر كمائة درهم وعشرة دنانير تساوي الصورة. وقد صرح به في من دليله من أن عند قوله «ملك نصاب حولي فارغ عن الدين قوله حتى إن من كان له مائة . درهم الخ) أفاد أن وجوب الضم إذا لم يكن كل واحد منهما نصاباً بأن كان أقل فأما إذا كان كل واحد
