البدائع إلى الإمام حيث الفضة قال : ثم عند أبي حنيفة يعتبر في التقويم منفعة الفقراء كما أصله حتى روي عنه أنه قال : إذا هو كان لرجل خمسة وتسعون درهماً ودينار يساوي خمسة دراهم أنه تجب الزكاة وذلك بأن تقوم بالذهب كل خمسة منها بدينار ا هـ .
His saying: (And the value of trade goods is added to the two currencies, and gold to silver by value. As for the first, because this conclusion is derived from their reasoning that the owner, just as he can buy for trade, can buy for personal use and gift—meaning it is not considered trade except by intention. And if he intended at the time of purchase to sell it along with his trade goods, then he has intended trade with it, unlike the mudarib as you know. As for the invalidity of his intending it as a subsidiary intent, it is denied; rather, his intention is valid for both, even if it enters as subsidiary. But the inclusion of a garment absolutely is denied, rather only garments of household use, and even if included, it is not determined; rather, if the seller wishes, he gives other than it, like its equivalent in clothing, as established in its place. His saying: (And it is mentioned in al-Mujtaba that debt in the middle of the year does not break...) its difference has preceded at the beginning of the book of zakat. The obligation in all is due to trade, even if the modes of counting differ. As for the second, it is for homogeneity in terms of being currency, and from this aspect it becomes a cause. And adding one of the two currencies to the other by value is the madhhab of the Imam, while according to the two disciples, the addition is by parts, and it is a narration from him. So that one who has one hundred and five dirhams and gold mithqals whose value is one hundred dirhams owes zakat according to him, contrary to them. They say: what is considered in them is quantity, not value, so that zakat is not due on jewelry whose weight is less than two hundred and whose value is more than that. And he says: addition is for homogeneity, and it is realized by considering value, not form, so one adds by it. And in al-Muhit: if one has one hundred dirhams and ten dinars whose value is less than one hundred, zakat is due according to them. And they differed on his statement, and the correct view is obligation because if completing the nisab of dirhams using the value of dinars is not possible, then completing the nisab of dinars using the value of dirhams is possible, because its value reaches ten dinars, so it is completed as a precaution for obligating zakat. End. And by this, the research of al-Zayla'i appears, transmitted with 'ten dinars', thinking that obligating zakat in this case according to the correct view is due to completeness of parts, not by value, but it is not as he thought; rather, the obligation is by value as indicated by the reasoning of al-Muhit. For its conclusion is considering value from each of the two currencies, not from one specifically. So if the nisab is not completed by considering the value of gold in silver, it is completed by considering the value of silver in gold. So how can it be a reasoning against considering value absolutely when the parts are complete, with the objection that if the value of one increases and the other does not decrease, like one hundred dirhams and ten dinars worth one hundred and eighty, then the consequence of his statement of not considering value when the parts are complete is that he only owes five (dirhams), but the apparent is that he owes seven, considering value. Because addition is only for homogeneity, and it is by meaning, which is value, not by form. And he explicitly stated in his proof from his saying: 'Whoever owns a full-year nisab free of debt' up to his saying: 'Even that one who has one hundred dirhams...' It indicates that addition is obligatory when each one is not a nisab, i.e., less. But if each one is a complete nisab without excess, addition is not obligatory, rather each should be paid its zakat. And if he adds one to the other to pay all from gold or silver, there is no harm according to us, but the valuation must be what is most beneficial for the poor in terms of circulation. Otherwise, he pays from each one-tenth of a tenth (i.e., 2.5%). If there is an increase on each of the two nisabs, then according to them (the two disciples), addition of one increase to the other is not obligatory because they obligate zakat on fractions proportionally. According to him (Abu Hanifa), if the increase from both reaches four mithqals and forty dirhams, then it is likewise (i.e., no addition); otherwise, addition of one increase to the other is obligatory to complete four mithqals and forty dirhams, because zakat is not obligatory on fractions according to him. Thus in al-Bada'i. His saying: (And the correct view is obligation) he attributed in al-Bada'i to the Imam where he said: Then according to Abu Hanifa, in valuation, the benefit of the poor is considered, as in its origin, until it is narrated from him: if a man has ninety-five dirhams and a dinar worth five dirhams, zakat is obligatory, because one values by gold, each five dirhams as one dinar. End.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage explains the second ruling from the matn: 'And the value of trade goods is added to the two currencies, and gold to silver by value.' The commentator first addresses why trade goods are added to gold and silver: it is because the basis for zakat on such goods is trade intention (niyyah al-tijarah). The owner can intend trade for any purchase, and if he purchases goods with the intention to sell them along with his trading stock, they become part of his trade assets. This is different from a mudarib (investment agent) who cannot independently intend trade with the capital. The commentator rejects the notion that such intention is invalid if it is subsidiary; rather, it is valid even if the item is also for personal use, as long as the primary purpose is trade. For example, a garment bought for one's own use but later intended for trade—if the seller chooses to give it as part of trade, it is acceptable. A cross-reference to the debt issue from the earlier passage is noted. The main focus then shifts to the rule of adding gold to silver. The commentator states the central intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that gold and silver are added together for zakat purposes by value (qimah), not by weight (ajza'). The two disciples (Sahibayn) say addition is by weight—meaning the actual number of coins or weight of metal. This difference leads to practical consequences. For example, if someone has 105 dirhams and gold worth 100 dirhams, according to Imam Abu Hanifa he owes zakat because the total value is 205 (exceeding 200 dirham nisab), but according to the Sahibayn, the gold weight may be less than 20 mithqal, so no nisab is reached in either category individually, so no zakat. The Sahibayn argue that the nisab for gold and silver is based on quantity (weight), not value, so a small amount of gold cannot be added to silver by value. Abu Hanifa argues that the reason for adding them is homogeneity (jins) — both are currencies (thaman) — and homogeneity is established by their function as monetary value, not by physical form. Therefore, the test is whether the combined value reaches the nisab of either currency. The commentator then cites al-Muhit: if someone has 100 dirhams and 10 dinars worth less than 100 dirhams, zakat is due according to both (Imam and Sahibayn) because the value of the dinars can be used to complete the nisab of dirhams or vice versa. The correct view (al-sahih) is that zakat is obligatory, based on the precautionary principle (ihtiyat) of completing the nisab by considering value from either side. The commentator refutes al-Zayla'i's interpretation that this case proves no value consideration; rather, it proves that value is considered from both sides. He gives an example: if one has 100 dirhams and 10 dinars worth 180 dirhams total, not adding value would yield only 5 dirhams zakat on the dirhams alone, but adding value yields 7 dirhams. The rule is then extended: if each type individually reaches a full nisab without surplus, no addition is needed; but if both have a surplus (e.g., gold above 20 mithqal, silver above 200 dirhams), then according to the Sahibayn, zakat on fractions is proportional (obligated on each excess separately), while according to Abu Hanifa, the surpluses must be added if they together reach a nisab (4 mithqal or 40 dirhams) because his school does not obligate zakat on fractions alone. Finally, the valuation should be to the benefit of the poor (anfa' li al-fuqara') meaning the most advantageous for them in terms of marketability. An example: 95 dirhams and a dinar worth 5 dirhams — the combined value reaches 100 dirhams, so zakat is due, and the payment can be made all in gold or silver.

Key Terms
الضم (al-damm): The legal process of combining different categories of zakatable wealth (gold, silver, trade goods) to complete the nisab.
الثمنين (al-thamanayn): The two currencies: gold and silver, considered as the standard of value.
المجانسة (al-mujanasa): Linguistic or legal homogeneity that justifies combining items for zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), how are gold and silver combined for zakat nisab?
A) By weight (parts)
B) By value
C) Not combined
D) Only if both are in coin form

Key Takeaway
Gold and silver are combined by value, not weight, because their monetary function creates legal homogeneity (jins), making zakat due when total value reaches the nisab of either.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate

Combining Gold and Silver for Zakat Nisab: Value vs Parts
Intra-Hanafi dispute on combining gold and silver by value or parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on combining gold and silver for zakat nisab, explain the basis of each, and apply the mufta bihi (value-based) ruling in a mixed-currency scenario.

Core Idea
Gold and silver are homogeneous by value; the reliant position combines them by market value.

Should gold and silver be treated as one category for zakat nisab, or are they separate? This is a classical intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa held that both are thaman (currency) and thus homogeneous, so they should be combined by their market value. His two main students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagreed: they maintained that each is a separate species and combination is only by quantity (weight or count). The later Hanafi school has adopted the Imam’s view as the mufta bihi. In this lesson, we examine the evidence, the practical differences, and how the tarjih was settled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Read the opening lines of the passage. Note that the text presents two positions: 'damm by value' for Imam Abu Hanifa, and 'damm by parts' for the Sahibayn. Also note that a narration from Imam himself agrees with the Sahibayn.
The intra-Hanafi nature means both views are within the school, but only one is relied upon.
2
Trace the proof for each view
For Imam: The basis is mujanisha (homogeneity) due to both being mediums of exchange (thamaniyya). This homogeneity is realized by value, not form. For the Sahibayn: What matters is the physical quantity (al-qadr), not value. Note the example of jewelry: no zakat if weight below nisab even if value exceeds.
The text quotes Al-Muhit and Al-Bada'i' for examples.
3
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Take a classic example: someone has 100 dirhams and 10 dinars worth 140 dirhams (1 dinar = 14 dirhams). Imam: combined value = 240 dirhams → zakat on total (6 dirhams). Sahibayn: gold (10 dinars) is a separate nisab? Actually 10 dinars = 1.19 mithqal? But example in text: 100 dirhams and 10 dinars worth 140 → each is a nisab? Wait, gold nisab is 20 dinars, so 10 dinars is half nisab. So Sahibayn do not combine, instead they treat each as separate: dirhams are full nisab, gold is half nisab, so zakat on dirhams only (2.5 dirhams) and nothing on gold because it's less than nisab. But the text says 'it is a complete nisab, half gold and half silver'? Actually that example is from Al-Muhit: 100 dirhams and 10 dinars worth 140 – Imam says 6 dirhams due (since total 240 × 2.5% = 6). Sahibayn say each is a nisab: gold nisab is 20 dinars, so 10 dinars = half nisab, but wait – the example says 'it is a complete nisab, half gold and half silver' – that suggests they consider the combination of both as a single nisab? Actually it means: the total is a complete nisab (since 240 dirhams), but each component is half the nisab. So Sahibayn say: each half pays zakat separately: 2.5% on the gold portion (value 140) = 3.5 dirhams? Wait, careful: gold zakat is 2.5% of its value, so 140 × 0.025 = 3.5; silver portion 100 dirhams × 0.025 = 2.5, total 6? That equals 6! But the text says 'in each half a quarter of its tenth' – that's 2.5% – so total is also 6. So in that example the result is same. The real difference appears when one component is less than half nisab. For example: 105 dirhams and gold worth 100 dirhams (total 205, a nisab). Imam: zakat on total (5.125 dirhams). Sahibayn: dirhams are nisab (200) + 5 excess, so zakat on 200 + proportion on excess? Actually they require parts: gold is separate – its weight must be at nisab? Gold nisab is 20 mithqal (approx 85g). The example with 'mithqals' – 5 mithqal of gold? Not clear. Better use simpler: 100 dirhams and a gold ornament worth 100 dirhams. This helps students see the difference.
The thamarah is clearest when each component alone is less than nisab but combined value exceeds nisab.
4
Identify the tarjih (preponderance)
The passage explicitly states 'al-sahih al-wujub' (the correct is obligation) based on the reasoning of Al-Muhit: if one cannot complete the silver nisab by gold's value, then complete the gold nisab by silver's value. This shows value is considered from both sides. Moreover, the benefit of the poor (manfa'at al-fuqara') supports the value-based view. Therefore, the mufta bihi is Imam's position.
The objection of al-Zayla'i is refuted: he mistakenly thought the corrected case was based on parts, but the text clarifies it is value.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Take a modern example: someone has $500 in cash (equivalent of silver) and a gold ring worth $400. Neither alone reaches nisab (nisab of silver ~$595, gold ~$5,000). According to the mufta bihi, combine by value: total $900 (exceeds silver nisab, but not gold nisab – does it matter?). Actually, the homogeneity means we compare to the lower nisab? The Hanafi school considers nisab of silver for cash, and combines gold at its value. So total value in silver terms ($900) exceeds silver nisab ($595) → zakat due on $900 at 2.5%. This is the practical application.
This illustrates 'value-based combination' in a contemporary setting.

And combining one of the two currencies to the other by value is the way of Imam Abu Hanifa, while according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is by parts, and it is a narration from him (Abu Hanifa). So if someone has one hundred and five dirhams and mithqals of gold worth one hundred dirhams, zakat is due according to him, contrary to them. They say: what matters in them is quantity, not value, so zakat is not due on jewelry weighing less than two hundred (dirhams) even if its value exceeds that. He says: the combination is due to homogeneity, which is realized by value, not form, so he combines by it. And they differed on his (Imam's) saying; the correct is obligation because if completing the nisab of dirhams by the value of the dinars is not possible, then completing the nisab of dinars by the value of the dirhams is possible since their value reaches ten dinars, so it is completed out of caution for making zakat obligatory. By this the discussion of al-Zayla'i appears, transmitted with 'ten dinars' mistakenly thinking that obligating zakat in this issue according to the correct view is due to completion of parts, not by value, and it is not as he thought. Rather, obligation is by value as indicated by the reasoning of al-Muhit. The summary of which is considering value from the aspect of each of the two currencies, not from the aspect of one of them specifically. So if the nisab is not completed by the value of gold with silver, it is completed by the value of silver with gold. So how can it be a reasoning for not considering value absolutely when the parts are complete? And there is an objection if the value of one increases and the value of the other does not decrease, like one hundred dirhams and ten dinars worth one hundred and eighty. Then the implication of his statement of not considering value when the parts are complete would be that only five is due, while the apparent is that seven is due considering value. Taking the combination is only for homogeneity, and that is by meaning, which is value, not by form. And it is explicitly stated in [؟] that it is from his proof that from when he said 'ownership of a nisab for a year free of debt'. His statement 'so that if someone has one hundred... dirhams etc.' indicates that the obligation of combining applies when each of them is not a full nisab, i.e., they are less. But if each of them is a complete nisab and there is no excess, combining is not obligatory. Rather, he should pay zakat from each one. If he combines one to the other and pays all from gold or silver, there is no harm according to us, but the valuation must be based on what is more beneficial for the poor in terms of circulation. Otherwise, he pays from each one a quarter of its tenth. According to him (Imam), it is considered: if the excess from both reaches four mithqals and forty dirhams, then it is the same; otherwise, combining one excess to the other is obligatory to complete four mithqals and forty dirhams because zakat is not obligatory on fractions according to him. Thus in al-Bada'i'. His statement: (And the correct is obligation) is attributed in al-Bada'i' to the Imam, where he said: Then according to Abu Hanifa, in valuation the benefit of the poor is considered as per his principle, so it is reported that he said: If a man has ninety-five dirhams and a dinar worth five dirhams, zakat is obligatory. That is by valuing with gold, every five of them as a dinar. End. And in al-Muhit he said: If he has one hundred dirhams and ten dinars worth one hundred and forty, then according to Abu Hanifa six dirhams are due, while according to them it is a complete nisab, half gold and half silver, so in each half a quarter of its tenth is due. And in it also: If he has one hundred and fifty dirhams and five dinars worth fifty, zakat is due by consensus. If he has a silver jug weighing one hundred and worth two hundred due to its craftsmanship, zakat is not due by value because quality and craftsmanship in ribawi commodities have no value when alone nor when exchanged with their own kind. End. And in al-Mi'raj: If he has one hundred and fifty dirhams and five dinars and the value of the dinars is not equal to fifty dirhams, zakat is due according to them. And the mashayikh differed on his (Imam's) saying: Some said: It is not due because combining is by value according to him, and he combines the smaller to the larger because the smaller follows the larger, so the nisab is not completed by it. And Faqih Abu Ja'far said: It is due according to his saying, and that is correct, and he combines the larger to the smaller. End. And it is evidence that there is no consideration of completing parts according to him; rather, he combines one currency to the other by value, and there is no difference between combining the smaller to the larger or vice versa.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about how to combine gold and silver when neither reaches the nisab threshold alone. The core issue is whether the combination is by value (qima) or by quantity (ajza). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the two currencies are homogeneous (mujanisha) due to their shared property of being mediums of exchange (thamaniyya), and this homogeneity is realized by value, not by form or weight. Therefore, he combines them based on market value. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that combination is by parts, i.e., by weight or count. This leads to practical differences: for example, if someone has 105 dirhams and some gold mithqals worth 100 dirhams, Imam says zakat is due because the combined value reaches 205 dirhams (nisab), while the Sahibayn say no because the gold quantity is less than the nisab for gold. The text then discusses various scenarios from al-Muhit and al-Mi'raj, including cases where the numbers are exact or have fractions. The relied-upon position (al-mufta bihi) is the Imam's view, but the Sahibayn's view is also considered. The commentary emphasizes that the ultimate criterion is the benefit of the poor (manfa'at al-fuqara'), as reported from Imam Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
ضم (damm): Combining two different types of wealth (gold and silver) for the purpose of completing the nisab for zakat
مجانسة (mujanisha): Homogeneity between gold and silver due to both being mediums of exchange (thamaniyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, when combining gold and silver for zakat nisab, what is the basis of combination?
A) By weight (ajza) only
B) By market value (qima)
C) By number of coins
D) By the larger of the two

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi combines gold and silver by market value because they share the 'illa of thamaniyya, ensuring zakat is obligatory when the total value reaches nisab.

Zakat — Almsgiving

Defining the 'Ashir and His Conditions
Definition of the tithe collector and conditions for his appointment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of the 'ashir and identify the four conditions for his valid appointment, explaining the rationale for each.

Core Idea
The 'ashir is a protector-collector; his appointment requires freedom, Islam, non-Hashimi status, and capability.

The term 'ashir (tithe collector) appears in classical fiqh texts with a specific technical meaning. It is not merely a tax collector but an official appointed by the Imam to collect zakat from traveling merchants while providing security. This lesson parses the definition and the essential conditions that make his appointment valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic origin
The word 'ashir is derived from 'ushr (tenth). Note that the collector may take varying rates depending on the merchant's status.
Lexically, it means taking a tenth, but in practice, the rate varies.
2
Understand the conventional usage in fiqh
The 'ashir is the person appointed by the Imam to collect sadaqat (zakat) from merchants on the road. The name 'ashir is retained even though he collects different percentages.
The term is conventional, not literal.
3
Extract the conditions for appointment
From the passage, list the four conditions: freedom (not a slave), Islam, non-Hashimi descent, and capability to protect the merchants.
These conditions are based on authority, trust, and the nature of the funds.
4
Analyze the reasoning behind each condition
A slave lacks authority (wilaya). A non-Muslim cannot rule over Muslims (Quranic evidence). A Hashimi cannot receive sadaqa (hadith). The collector must be able to protect to justify the collection.
Each condition has a distinct dalil (evidence) mentioned in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العاشر وهو فاعل من عشرته أعشره عشراً ، بالضم والمراد هنا ما يدور اسم العشر في متعلق أخذه فإنه إنما يأخذ العشر من الحربي لا المسلم ، والذمي أو تسمية للشيء باعتبار بعض أحواله العشر من الحربي من المسلم والذمي والأدوار مركب فيتعسر التلفظ به والعشر منفرد فلا يتعسر قوله (هو من نصبه الإمام ليأخذ الصدقات من التجار أي من نصبه الإمام على الطريق ليأخذ الصدقات من التجار المارين بأموالهم عليه قالوا وإنما ينصب ليأمن التجار من اللصوص ويحميهم منهم فيستفاد منه أنه لا بد أن يكون قادراً على الحماية لأن الجباية بالحماية ولذا قال في الغاية : ويشترط في العامل أن يكون حراً مسلماً غير هاشمي، فلا يكون عبداً لعدم الولاية، ولا يصح أن يكون كافراً لأنه لا يلي على المسلم بالآية، ولا يصح أن يكون هاشمياً لما روي أن الصدقة لا تحل لآل محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم. وقوله (والمراد هنا ما يدور اسم العشر الخ بيانه ما في النهاية : العاشر لغة من عشرت القوم أعشرهم بالضم عشراً مضمومة إذا أخذت أموالهم، فعلى هذا تسمية العاشر الذي يأخذ العشر إنما يستقيم على أخذه من الحربي لا من المسلم والذمي لأنه يأخذ من المسلم ربع العشر، ومن الذمي نصف العشر، ومن الحربي العشر على ما يجيء، ولكن في حق كل واحد منهم يدور اسم
Chapter of the 'Ashir. It is the active participle from 'I gave him a tenth: I give him a tenth by doubling the 'ayn. And what is meant here is the name 'tenth' circulates in the context of what he takes. So he only takes the tenth from the harbi, not from the Muslim and the dhimmi. Or it is naming something by considering some of its states: the tenth from the harbi, from the Muslim and the dhimmi, and the roles are complex, making pronunciation difficult. So the single word 'tenth' is not difficult. His statement: (He is the one whom the Imam appoints to take the sadaqat from the merchants.) Meaning he is appointed by the Imam on the road to take sadaqat from merchants passing by with their goods. They said: He is only appointed to make merchants safe from thieves and to protect them from them. So it is understood that he must be capable of protection, because collection is through protection. Hence it is stated in al-Ghaya: Conditions for the worker are that he be free, Muslim, and non-Hashimi. He cannot be a slave because he lacks authority. He cannot be a disbeliever because he cannot have authority over a Muslim by the verse. He cannot be a Hashimi based on the narration that sadaqa is not permissible for the family of Muhammad (peace be upon him). And his statement: (What is meant here is the name 'tenth' circulates...) its clarification is as in al-Nihaya: lexically, 'ashir is from 'I gave the people a tenth: I give them a tenth' meaning I took their wealth. Thus, the naming of the collector who takes the tenth is only correct for his taking from the harbi, not from the Muslim and the dhimmi, because he takes a quarter of a tenth from the Muslim, half a tenth from the dhimmi, and a tenth from the harbi as will come. But regarding each of them, the name circulates.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on the 'ashir (tithe collector) in Islamic law. The term 'ashir is derived from 'ushr (tenth), but the official appointed by the Imam may take different rates from different groups: a tenth from hostile non-Muslims (harbi), half a tenth from dhimmis, and a quarter of a tenth from Muslims. The name 'ashir is retained because the rate of a tenth is the most distinctive. The passage then explains the conditions for the appointment: the 'ashir must be free (not a slave) because a slave lacks the authority to enforce collection; he must be Muslim because a non-Muslim cannot have authority over a Muslim; and he must not be a Hashimi (descendant of the Prophet's clan) because sadaqa (including zakat) is impermissible for them. Additionally, the 'ashir must be capable of providing protection to the merchants, as the justification for the tax is the security he provides. This sets out the legal prerequisites for a valid appointment.

Key Terms
العاشر (al-'ashir): The officer appointed by the Imam to collect zakat from merchants passing by on trade routes
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is not under Islamic protection and is in a state of war with Muslims; from them, a full tenth is taken

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He must be a Hashimi
D) He must be capable of protecting merchants

Key Takeaway
The 'ashir must be free, Muslim, non-Hashimi, and capable of protecting merchants; otherwise his appointment is invalid.

Definition and Conditions of the 'Ashir
Defines the 'Ashir and his conditions of appointment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the 'Ashir and state the three conditions required for his appointment, explaining the rationale for each.

Core Idea
The 'Ashir is a zakat official whose appointment must meet three conditions: free, Muslim, and non-Hashimi.

This lesson introduces the 'Ashir, an official appointed by the Imam to collect zakat from merchants' trade goods. We will explore the definition, etymology, and especially the three essential conditions for his appointment: freedom, Islam, and non-Hashimi status. The source explains the reasoning behind each condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Extract the core definition of the 'Ashir from the text: he is the one appointed by the Imam to take alms from merchants.
The term 'Ashir is derived from 'ushr (tithe).
2
Examine the etymology
Note why he is called 'Ashir even though he collects varying fractions: the name revolves around the concept of tithe.
3

From the al-Ghayah quote, identify the three conditions: free (not a slave), Muslim, and non-Hashimi.
A fourth condition, capability of protection, is derived from the purpose of the appointment.
4

5

Chapter of the 'Ashir: He is the one appointed by the Imam to take alms (zakat) from merchants. He is the active participle from 'I 'ashartuhu a'shiruhu 'ashran' with dammah. What is meant here is that the name 'tithe' revolves around what he takes, because he only takes the tithe from a harbi, not from a Muslim and not from a dhimmi; or it is a naming of the thing by considering some of its states. Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the 'Ashir and his conditions. The 'Ashir is an official appointed by the Imam to collect zakat from merchants' trade goods as they pass on the road. The name 'Ashir derives from 'ushr (tithe), even though he may collect different fractions (e.g., 1/4 from Muslims, 1/2 from dhimmis, full from harbis). هاشمي (hāshimī): A descendant of Hashim, the Prophet's clan, who are prohibited from receiving or handling zakat.

Zakat on Mixed Gold and Silver: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ruling on zakat for mixed gold and silver assets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on joining gold and silver for zakat nisab and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Gold and silver are joined by value for nisab, but how the lesser is treated divides Hanafi imams.

When a person owns both gold and silver, and the combined value reaches the nisab threshold, does zakat become obligatory? The Hanafi school agrees it does, but a nuanced internal dispute concerns how the two metals are combined. This lesson unpacks the positions of Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and later Hanafi authorities to clarify the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the disagreement is about the method of 'joining' (ḍamm) gold and silver for nisab calculation—by weight/quantity or by market value.
2
State Abu Hanifa’s view
Abu Hanifa joins by value and treats the lesser metal as subordinate to the greater; if the lesser does not complete the nisab of the greater, it is ignored.
Example: 150 dirhams + 5 dinars worth <50 dirhams may not trigger zakat according to this interpretation.
3
State the Sahibayn’s view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider the mixed assets as a complete nisab of both types, each paying zakat on its own half, regardless of individual nisab.
Example: 100 dirhams + 10 dinars worth 140 dirhams gives a 240 total; they treat it as half gold half silver.
4
Note later Hanafi ikhtilaf
Later scholars differed on how to apply Abu Hanifa’s principle. Some held that the lesser cannot complete the nisab, others (like Abu Ja‘far) said the greater can be joined to the lesser.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (sahih) is that zakat is due when the combined value reaches nisab, and the direction of joining (lesser to greater or vice versa) does not matter.
This is the position for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن قال لم يتم الحول أو على المحيط فقال: لو كان له مائة درهم وعشرة دنانير قيمتها مائة وأربعون، فعند أبي حنيفة تجب ستة دراهم وعندهما هو نصاب تام نصفه ذهب ونصفه فضة فيجب في كل نصف ربع عشره. وفيه أيضاً لو كان له مائة وخمسون درهماً وخمسة دنانير قيمتها خمسون تجب الزكاة بالإجماع. ولو كان له إبريق فضة وزنه مائة وقيمته لصناعته مائتان لا تجب الزكاة باعتبار القيمة لأن الجودة والصنعة في أموال الربا لا قيمة لها عند انفرادها ولا عند المقابلة بجنسها. اهـ. وفي المعراج: لو كان له مائة وخمسون درهماً وخمسة دنانير وقيمة الدنانير لا تساوي خمسين درهماً تجب الزكاة على قولهما. واختلف المشايخ على قوله قال بعضهم: لا تجب لأن الضم باعتبار القيمة عنده ويضم الأقل إلى الأكثر لأن الأقل تابع للأكثر فلا يكمل النصاب به. وقال الفقيه أبو جعفر: تجب على قوله وهو الصحيح ويضم الأكثر إلى الأقل. اهـ. وهو دليل على أنه لا اعتبار بتكامل الأجزاء عنده وإنما يضم أحد النقدين إلى الآخر قيمة، ولا فرق بين ضم الأقل إلى الأكثر أو عكسه.
If someone says the year has not completed, or according to al-Muhit he said: If he has one hundred dirhams and ten dinars worth one hundred and forty, then according to Abu Hanifa six dirhams are obligatory, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is a complete nisab, half gold and half silver, so one quarter of a tenth is obligatory on each half. Also in it: If he has one hundred and fifty dirhams and five dinars worth fifty, zakat is obligatory by consensus. If he has a silver jug weighing one hundred and valued at two hundred for its craftsmanship, zakat is not obligatory by consideration of value because quality and craftsmanship in ribawi wealth have no value when isolated nor when exchanged for their own kind. End. In al-Mi'raj: If he has one hundred and fifty dirhams and five dinars, and the dinars' value is less than fifty dirhams, zakat is obligatory according to them (the two disciples). The mashayikh differed concerning his (Abu Hanifa's) statement: Some said it is not obligatory because joining (ضم) is by value according to him, and the lesser is joined to the greater because the lesser is subordinate to the greater, so nisab is not completed thereby. Abu Ja'far said: It is obligatory according to his statement, and that is correct, and the greater is joined to the lesser. End. This indicates that he does not consider the completeness of parts (تكامل الأجزاء), but rather joins one currency to the other by value, and there is no difference between joining the lesser to the greater or vice versa.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding zakat on mixed assets of gold and silver. The matn of Kanz mentions that if the owner claims the year has not completed, the commentator quotes from al-Muhit who gives examples. The ruling depends on whether the different types (gold and silver) are considered together for nisab. Abu Hanifa holds that they are joined by value, but only if the combined value reaches the nisab of one type? The text: if someone has 100 dirhams and 10 dinars worth 140 dirhams (total value 240? Actually 100 + 140 = 240? Wait, it says 'ten dinars worth 140' so total value 100+140=240, but nisab of silver is 200 dirhams. Abu Hanifa says 6 dirhams due, which is 1/40 of the 240? Actually 1/40 of 240 = 6, so he treats the whole as silver nisab? The Sahibayn say it's a complete nisab, half gold half silver, so each half pays 1/40 of its value. So consistent. Then another example: 150 dirhams and 5 dinars worth 50, total 200 - consensus that zakat is due. Then a silver jug: weight 100 dirhams, value 200 due to craftsmanship. Hanafi rule: for ribawi items (gold/silver), craftsmanship value is ignored if exchanged for same species (like for like), so zakat is not due on the jug because its actual silver weight is below nisab. Then a nuanced case: if value of dinars is less than equivalent to dirhams for nisab, the Sahibayn still require zakat because they consider the types together regardless of value? Actually the dispute is about the method of joining (ضم): Abu Hanifa joins by value and considers the lesser subordinate to the greater, so if the lesser does not complete nisab, it is ignored. The Sahibayn say the types are considered together irrespective of value, so even if the dinars' value is less than the dirham nisab equivalent, zakat is due because the total quantity of both species may reach nisab? The text is not entirely clear but the mashayikh differ on Abu Hanifa's position. The relied-upon view (sahih) is that zakat is due and the greater is joined to the lesser (i.e., the dominant value determines). The final conclusion: the completeness of parts is not considered; rather one currency is joined to the other by value, and it makes no difference whether lesser is joined to greater or vice versa.

Key Terms
ضم (ḍamm): Joining or combining different assets (gold and silver) for the purpose of calculating nisab.
ربع العشر (rub‘ al-‘ushr): One-quarter of a tenth, i.e., 2.5% – the standard zakat rate on gold and silver.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Zakat is only on the gold

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that gold and silver are joined by value for nisab, making zakat obligatory when the combined value reaches the nisab threshold.

The Owner's Oath in Zakat Claims
Exegesis of a matn on owner's claims regarding zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three claims for which the owner's oath is accepted, identify the exception for livestock, and explain the principle of joining same-type assets (al-damm) in nisab calculation.

Core Idea
The owner's oath is accepted for claims that would negate zakat, but not for self-payment of livestock zakat.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains when a zakat owner's claim is accepted with his oath. The 'Ashir (collector) must rely on the owner's word, but there are limits. Understanding these limits prevents misuse and ensures zakat is collected properly. We will examine the three accepted claims and the crucial exception for livestock, plus a subtle rule about combining assets of the same type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three accepted claims
Read the matn phrase: 'so whoever says: “The year has not completed” or “I have a debt” or “I paid it myself” or “to another 'Ashir” and he swears, he is truthful.' List the three distinct claims that discharge the owner's liability.
Note that 'paid to another 'Ashir' is a variant of the third claim.
2
Understand the exception for livestock
Find the exception clause: 'except in the case of livestock (sa'ima) when he pays himself.' This means the owner cannot escape liability by claiming self-payment for grazing livestock. Identify why this exception exists (the 'Ashir has no authority over livestock; it must be paid by the owner directly).
Livestock zakat is collected by the Sa'i, not the 'Ashir.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind accepting the claims
The commentator explains that these claims are denials of obligation. Since the 'Ashir's authority depends on zakat being due, if the owner's claim negates that obligation, it is accepted with oath. This is the core legal logic.
4
Examine the 'damm' (joining) principle
The passage mentions that denying the year's completion refers to what is in his hand and what is in his house. If he has other house-held wealth of the same type that has completed a year, the 'Ashir must combine them (damm) for nisab, unless a preventer exists. Work through an example: a trader has 150 dirhams in hand (incomplete haul) and 50 dirhams at home (complete haul). Identical type, so total 200 dirhams (exceeds nisab) => zakat is due despite the haul claim.
This prevents artificial splitting of assets to avoid zakat.
5
Apply the qualification on debt
The last sentence restricts 'debt' to debts owed to people (not to God, such as unpaid zakat). Confirm the implication: if the debt is a financial obligation to another person, it reduces nisab and can be claimed; if it is a duty to God, it does not affect the current zakat obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فمن قال لم يتم الحول أو على دين أو أديت أنا أو إلى عاشر آخر وحلف صدق إلا في السوائم في دفعه بنفسه) أما الأول والثاني لإنكاره الوجوب، وقدمنا أن شرط ولاية الأخذ وجود الزكاة، فكل ما وجوده مسقط فالحكم كذلك إذا ادعاه. والمراد بنفي تمام الحول نفيه عما في يده وما في بيته لأنه لو كان في بيته مال آخر قد حال عليه الحول وما مر به لم يحل عليه الحول واتحد الجنس فإن العاشر لا يلتفت إليه لوجوب الضم في متحد الجنس إلا لمانع كما قدمناه. وقيد في المعراج الدين بدين العباد.
His statement: (So whoever says: 'The year has not completed' or 'I have a debt', or 'I paid it myself' or 'to another 'Ashir', and he swears, he is truthful, except in the case of livestock (sa'ima) when he pays himself.) As for the first and second, it is denial of obligation. We have presented that the condition for the authority to take is the existence of zakat. So anything whose existence is a ground for dropping (zakat) – the ruling is likewise when he claims it. What is meant by denying completion of the year is denying it regarding what is in his hand and what is in his house, because if he has other wealth in his house on which the year has passed, and what he passed with has not completed the year, and the type is the same, then the 'Ashir does not pay attention to it because joining (ḍamm) is obligatory in the same type unless there is a hindrance, as we have presented. And in al-Mi'raj, 'debt' is restricted to a debt owed to people.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn phrase regarding the owner's oath. The owner may claim that zakat is not due for one of three reasons: the haul has not completed a year, he is in debt, or he has already paid zakat (to the state or to another collector). He is to be believed upon taking an oath. The exception is livestock (sa'ima) which must be paid by the owner himself (i.e., he cannot avoid it by claiming he paid). The commentator explains: claims of incomplete haul and debt are denials of obligation; since the authority of the 'Ashir to collect depends on zakat being due, if the owner's claim would negate the obligation, it is accepted with oath. The denial of the year's completion refers to what is in his possession and in his home; if he has other wealth at home that already reached a year but the goods he is carrying have not, and they are of the same type (e.g., both silver), the 'Ashir considers the total due to the principle of joining (ḍamm) within the same species, unless there is an obstacle. This is a nuanced detail. Also, debt is restricted to debts owed to people (not to God, like previous zakat).

Key Terms
السوائم (as-sawā'im): Grazing livestock (cattle, camels, sheep) subject to zakat collected by the Sa'i.
الضم في متحد الجنس (aḍ-ḍamm fī muttaḥid al-jins): Combining assets of the same type from different locations for nisab calculation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which case is the owner's claim that he paid zakat not accepted with his oath?
A) When he claims the haul did not complete a year
B) When he claims he paid to another 'Ashir
C) When he has livestock (sa'ima) and claims he paid himself
D) When he claims he has a debt

Key Takeaway
The owner's oath is a valid discharge for most claims, but the livestock exception and the damm principle ensure zakat is not evaded through false statements or asset splitting.

Apparent and Hidden Wealth in Zakat: The Hanafi Classification
Defines apparent and hidden wealth with proofs and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dhahir (apparent) and batin (hidden) zakatable wealth and identify which the imam may collect.

Core Idea
Zakatable wealth is divided into dhahir (visible) and batin (hidden); the imam collects dhahir.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi classification of zakatable wealth into two categories: apparent wealth (al-mal al-dhahir) and hidden wealth (al-mal al-batin). Understanding this distinction is key to knowing when the imam or his agents have the authority to collect zakat directly, and when the owner is responsible for paying it themselves. The primary proof is the Quranic command 'Take from their wealth a charity'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of zakatable wealth
Read the first lines of the passage where the author states 'Zakatable wealth is of two types: apparent... and hidden'. Note the examples given for each.
2
Determine the basis for the imam's authority over apparent wealth
Look at the proofs: the Quranic verse, the practice of the Prophet and caliphs, and the need for protection. Understand why hidden wealth is exempt from direct collection.
3
List the conditions for collection
Extract the conditions mentioned: existence of protection from the imam, obligation of zakat, visibility of wealth, and presence of owner.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a trader passing the 'ashir with livestock. This is apparent wealth, so the imam's agent may collect. If the owner declares hidden gold, the agent does not take it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصله أن مال الزكاة نوعان : ظاهر وهو المواشي والمال الذي يمر به التاجر على العاشر، وباطن وهو الذهب والفضة وأموال التجارة في مواضعها . أما الظاهر فللإمام ونوابه وهم المصدقون والعشار ولاية الأخذ للآية خذ من أموالهم صدقة [التوبة : ١٠٣] ولجعله للعاملين عليها حقاً فلو لم يكن للإمام مطالبتهم لم يكن له وجه ولما اشتهر من بعثه عليه الصلاة والسلام للقبائل لأخذ الزكاة وكذا الخلفاء بعده حتى قاتل الصديق مانعي الزكاة، ولا شك أن السوائم تحتاج إلى الحماية لأنها تكون في البراري بحماية السلطان وغيرها من الأموال إذا أخرجه في السفر احتاج إلى الحماية بخلاف الأموال الباطنة إذا لم يخرجها مالكها من المصر لفقد هذا المعنى. وفي البدائع وشرط ولاية الأخذ وجود الحماية من الإمام فلا شيء لو غلب الخوارج على مصر أو قرية وأخذوا منهم الصدقات ومنها وجوب الزكاة لأن المأخوذ زكاة فيراعي شرائطها كلها ومنها ظهور المال وحضور المالك فلو حضر وأخبر بما في بيته أو حضر ما له مع مستبضع نحوه فلا أخذ . وفي التبيين : إن هذا العمل مشروع وما ورد من ذم العشار محمول على من يأخذ أموال الناس ظلماً كما تفعله الظلمة اليوم. روي أن عمر أراد أن يستعمل أنس بن مالك على هذا العمل فقال له : أتستعملني على المكس فقال : ألا ترضى أن أقلدك ما قلد نبي رسول الله .اهـ. وفي الخانية قسم الجبايات والمؤن بين الناس على السوية يكون مأجوراً اهـ.
The summary: Zakatable wealth is of two types: apparent (dhahir) which is livestock and wealth that passes a trader by the 'ashir; and hidden (batin) which is gold, silver, and trade goods in their places. As for apparent, the imam and his deputies, who are the musaddiqun and 'ushshar, have the authority to take it, due to the verse 'Take from their wealth a charity' (9:103) and because He made a right for the workers over it. So if the imam had no right to demand it from them, he would have no basis, and due to the well-known sending of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to the tribes to take zakat, and likewise the caliphs after him, until al-Siddiq fought the preventers of zakat. There is no doubt that livestock need protection because they are in the wilderness with the protection of the sultan, and other wealth when taken out on a journey needs protection, unlike hidden wealth if its owner does not take it out of the city, due to the absence of this meaning. In al-Bada'i': And a condition for the authority to take is the existence of protection from the imam, so nothing is due if the Khawarij overpower a city or village and take the sadaqat from them. And among the conditions: the obligation of zakat because what is taken is zakat, so all its conditions are observed; and among them: the appearance of the wealth and the presence of the owner, so if he is present and informs about what is in his house, or his wealth is present with a partner or the like, then nothing is taken. In al-Tabyin: This work is legislated, and what is reported condemning the 'ushshar is interpreted as those who take people's wealth unjustly, as the oppressors do today. It is narrated that 'Umar wanted to appoint Anas ibn Malik for this work, and he said to him: 'Do you appoint me over the tax?' He said: 'Are you not satisfied that I delegate to you what the Messenger of Allah delegated to him?' And in al-Khaniyya: Dividing the collections and expenses among the people equally is rewarded.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the classification of zakatable wealth and the authority of the imam and his agents. The author first distinguishes between apparent wealth (dhahir) – which includes livestock and trade goods that pass by the 'ashir – and hidden wealth (batin) – gold, silver, and trade goods kept within the city. For apparent wealth, the imam and his deputies have the authority to collect it, supported by the Quranic verse 'Take from their wealth a charity' (9:103) and the practice of the Prophet and the caliphs. A crucial condition for this authority is the existence of protection (himaya) from the imam; if the Khawarij overpower an area and collect zakat, no further collection is due. Other conditions include the obligation of zakat itself (so the collectors must ensure all conditions of zakat are met) and the visibility of the wealth and presence of the owner. If the owner is present and declares what he has, or his wealth is with a partner, the 'ashir does not take anything until the conditions are verified. The passage also addresses the negative reputation of 'ushshar, clarifying that the criticism is directed at those who collect unjustly, not the legitimate collectors. The incident with 'Umar and Anas ibn Malik shows the legitimacy of the office. Finally, it notes that equally dividing the collected funds among the people is meritorious.

Key Terms
المال الظاهر (al-māl al-ẓāhir): Zakatable wealth that is visible, such as livestock and trade goods passing a customs post.
العاشر (al-‘āshir): The official collector of zakat on apparent wealth, originally collecting a tenth (ushr).
المصدق (al-muṣaddiq): A collector of sadaqah (zakat), synonymous with 'ashir but with emphasis on truthfulness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered apparent (dhahir) wealth for the purpose of imam's collection?
A) Gold coins kept in a safe at home
B) Silver jewelry worn by the owner
C) Livestock grazing in a field under imam's protection
D) Trade goods stored in a warehouse inside the city

Key Takeaway
The imam's authority to collect zakat directly is limited to apparent wealth (dhahir) such as livestock and trade goods passing the 'ashir, based on the Quranic command and the need for protection.

Conditions for Zakat Collection and the Legitimacy of the 'Ashir
Details conditions for collection and clarifies the legitimacy of the 'ashir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the imam's collection is valid and explain why the 'ashir is not inherently blameworthy.

Core Idea
The imam's collection of zakat is conditioned on his protection; collectors are legitimate unless they oppress.

This lesson focuses on the practical conditions that must be met for the imam's agents to lawfully collect zakat, as well as the clarification regarding the negative reputation of 'ushshar. The text emphasizes that collection is only valid under the imam's protection, and that collectors who take unjustly are the ones condemned, not the legitimate system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for the imam's authority to take zakat
Look for the statement 'شرط ولاية الأخذ وجود الحماية من الإمام' and the other conditions mentioned.
2
Understand the scenario when Khawarij take over
If the Khawarij collect zakat from the people, the imam cannot collect it again. This is because the condition of protection is missing for the imam.
3
Examine the condition of owner presence and wealth visibility
The text says if the owner is present and informs about hidden wealth, nothing is taken. This reinforces the limitation to apparent wealth.
4
Analyze the defense of the 'ashir
Read the part from al-Tabyin and the story of 'Umar and Anas. Understand that the condemnation is only for unjust collectors, not the office itself.

When the 'Ashir Believes the Claim: Incomplete Year, Debt, and Payment to Another 'Ashir
Explains when the 'ashir accepts claims of non-obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three claims for which the 'ashir accepts the oath and identify the exception for livestock.

Core Idea
The 'ashir believes the passer-by who swears that zakat is not due—unless the claim is personal payment of livestock zakat.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the procedural rules for the 'ashir (zakat collector) when a person passing by claims that zakat is not obligatory on his wealth. The matn lists four scenarios where the claim is accepted with an oath, but immediately carves out an exception for livestock if the person claims to have paid it himself. The commentary expands on the meaning of each claim and the rationale for the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four claims in the matn
Read the matn: 'Whoever says the year has not completed, or he has a debt, or I have paid personally, or to another 'ashir, and he swears, he is believed except regarding livestock in paying it personally.' Note the four claims: incomplete year, debt, personal payment, payment to another 'ashir.
The 'personal payment' claim is later restricted: it is only accepted if it refers to payment to the poor in the city or if there was another 'ashir.
2
Understand the livestock exception
The exception applies only to the claim of personal payment of livestock (sawa'im) zakat. The reason: the right to collect livestock zakat belongs to the sultan, so the person cannot nullify it by private payment.
For other claims (incomplete year, debt, payment to another 'ashir), the livestock exception does not apply; the person is believed with oath.
3
Recognise the requirement of oath
For all accepted claims, the person must swear an oath. The commentary justifies this later by the 'ashir's human right attached to the collection.
The oath is a condition for belief, except that for livestock personal payment, even with oath he is not believed.
4
Distinguish between types of 'personal payment'
The claim 'I paid personally' is only believed if it refers to payment in the city (m) to the poor, or if there was another 'ashir that year. Payment after leaving the city is not accepted. For livestock, personal payment is never accepted.
The condition of another 'ashir ensures the claim is plausible.

البيان الخ قال في الشرنبلالية لا يخفى ما فيه من معارضة المنطوق بالمفهوم فليتأمل اهـ. وفيه نظر لأنه لم يكتف بمفهوم كلام المصنف بل بما ينقله عن المعراج لكن عبارة المعراج بعد نقله عبارة الخبازية هكذا وقيل : ينبغي أن يصدقه فيما ينقص النصاب به لأنه لا يأخذ من المال الذي يكون أقل من النصاب لأن ما يأخذه العاشر زكاة حتى شرطت فيه شرائط الزكاة ذكره في شرح مختصر الكرخي للقدوري ا هـ . وهو صريح
The author's saying: 'Whoever says the year has not completed, or he has a debt, or I have paid personally, or to another 'ashir, and he swears, he is believed except regarding livestock in paying it personally.' As for the first and second, they are denial of obligation. We have stated that a condition of the authority to take is the existence of zakat, so whatever negates its existence drops it, so the ruling is thus if he claims it. The meaning of denying the completion of the year is denying it regarding what is in his hand and what is in his house, because if there is other wealth in his house for which the year has passed, and the wealth he passed with has not completed the year, and the type is the same, then the 'ashir does not pay attention to that because of the obligation to combine (damm) in the same type unless there is a preventing factor as we have stated. In al-Mi'raj, debt is restricted to debts owed to people (diyan al-'ibad), and we have stated that from it is the debt of zakat. The author (al-Musannif) used the word 'ushr (tenth) even if it is accompanied by something else, so it is permissible to call him an 'ashir (tithe collector). And his saying 'and naming the thing' is another response from the author of al-'Inaya. In al-Nahr from al-Sa'diyya: There is no need for that; rather, 'ushr is a term for what the 'ashir takes, whether what is taken is literally a tenth, a quarter, or half. Regarding debt, it includes debt that consumes (yastaghriq) the wealth and that which reduces the nisab, and this is correct. By this is refuted what is in Ghayat al-Bayan of restriction to what encumbers his wealth, and what is in al-Khabbaziyya that the 'ashir asks him about the amount of debt according to the correct view, so if he informs him of something that consumes the nisab, he believes him; otherwise he does not. Because the reducer is a preventer of obligation, so there is no difference, as in al-Mi'raj. The author indicated that the passer-by, if he says 'There is no sadaqah in this wealth,' he is believed with his oath, as in al-Mabsut, even if he does not explain the reason for negation. And in it also: If the trader informs the 'ashir that his goods are Marwazi or Herawi, and the 'ashir suspects him and there is harm in it for him, he makes him swear and takes the sadaqah according to his statement, because he has no authority to harm him. It is reported that 'Umar said to his workers: 'Do not search people's goods.' As for the third (claim of payment), because he claims to have placed the trust in its place. Its meaning is if there was another 'ashir in that year; otherwise he is not believed because of the evident lie. Its meaning also is if he paid personally in the city to the poor, because payment was delegated to him there, and the authority of taking is by passing (due to entering under protection). Because if he claimed to have paid personally to them after leaving the city, it is not accepted. He is not believed in his saying 'I paid personally the sadaqah of livestock to the poor' in the city, because the right of taking belongs to the sultan, so he does not have the power to nullify it, unlike hidden wealth. Then it is said: The first is zakat and the second is policy (siyasa). And it is said: The second is that the first is converted to nafl (supererogatory). This is the correct view, as in al-Hidayah. The apparent meaning of 'converted to nafl' is that if the imam does not take from him due to knowing that he paid to the poor, his liability is discharged religiously (diyanatan), and there is a difference of scholars, as in al-Mi'raj. In Jami' Abi al-Yusr: If the imam permits his payment, there is no harm, because if the imam initially permitted him to give to the poor personally, it is permissible, so similarly if he permits after giving. He swears, even though acts of worship are believed without oath, because a right of a human (the 'ashir) is attached in taking, and he claims against him a meaning that if he admits it, it would be due against him, so he swears hoping for nukul (refusal to swear) – unlike the hadd of qadhf, because judgment by nukul is not possible in hadd punishments as known, and unlike prayer and fasting because there is no one to accuse him in them. So the saying of Abu Yusuf that he should not swear because it is an act of worship is refuted. The author indicated by being satisfied with the oath that it is not required to produce a receipt (al-bara'a) when claiming payment to another 'ashir, following al-Jami' al-Saghir, because handwriting resembles handwriting, so it is not considered a sign, and this is the apparent report as in al-Bada'i'. But he required it in al-Asl because he claimed, and his claim has a sign, so he must produce it. In al-Mi'raj: Then according to those who require producing a receipt, is the oath required with it? There is disagreement. The author's saying: 'By this is refuted what is in Ghayat al-Bayan...' He said in al-Sharnabalaliyya: It is not hidden that there is a conflict between the explicit wording and the implied meaning, so let one ponder. And there is consideration because he did not rely on the implication of the author's words but on what he transmitted from al-Mi'raj. However, the phrase of al-Mi'raj after quoting the phrase of al-Khabbaziyya is as follows: 'And it is said: He should believe him in what reduces the nisab because he does not take wealth that is less than the nisab, because what the 'ashir takes is zakat, so the conditions of zakat are also required for it.' Mentioned in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Karkhi by al-Quduri. And this is explicit.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rules for when a person passing by the 'ashir claims that zakat is not due. The matn states: if a person says the year hasn't completed, or he has a debt, or he already paid himself or to another 'ashir, and he swears, he is believed—except for livestock (sawa'im) where he is not believed if he claims to have paid personally. The commentary expands each point: For claims of incomplete year, the denial refers to wealth in hand, not other wealth in his house of the same type that may have completed the year (due to the rule of combining). For debt, it includes debt that reduces or consumes the nisab, and the 'ashir should believe the person if he states such debt. For claims of payment, the person is believed with an oath if there was another 'ashir that year, or if he paid in the city to the poor. However, for livestock, the right of collection belongs to the sultan, so personal payment is not accepted unless the imam permits it. The text also addresses why an oath is required despite zakat being an act of worship: because the 'ashir has a human right attached. Abu Yusuf's opposing view (no oath needed) is refuted. Additionally, the passage discusses whether a receipt (bara'a) is required when claiming payment to another 'ashir; the relied-upon view is that it is not required, though Imam Muhammad required it in al-Asl. Finally, a note on the phrase 'by this is refuted...' shows a scholarly debate about the interpretation of debt reducing the nisab.

Key Terms
الحول (al-ḥawl): The passing of one lunar year, a condition for zakat obligation on wealth.
الدين (al-dayn): A debt that reduces or consumes the nisab, thereby preventing zakat obligation.
السوائم (al-sawā'im): Grazing livestock kept for their produce, on which zakat is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because livestock zakat is not obligatory.
B) Because the right to collect livestock zakat belongs to the sultan, so personal payment cannot nullify it.
C) Because he must pay to the 'ashir directly.
D) Because livestock owners are always dishonest.

Key Takeaway
The 'ashir accepts claims of incomplete year, debt, or payment to another 'ashir with an oath, but the claim of personal payment of livestock zakat is never accepted due to the sultan's right.

Zakat — Almsgiving

12 min
The Oath in Zakat Claims: Human Right vs. Act of Worship
Explains the rationale for oath and intra-Hanafi difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rationale for requiring an oath in zakat claims and evaluate Abu Yusuf's opposing view.

Core Idea
Zakat is worship, but the 'ashir's human right makes the oath necessary—unlike prayer, where no one can accuse.

While the matn establishes that the person is believed with an oath, a question arises: why is an oath needed if zakat is an act of worship? Abu Yusuf argued that no oath is needed. The commentary refutes this by distinguishing the 'ashir's claim of a human right (haqq al-'abd) from purely devotional acts. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and also touches on the issue of requiring a receipt.

Guided Path
1
Understand Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf held that no oath is required because zakat is an act of worship (ibadah), and acts of worship are accepted without oath (like prayer and fasting).
This is a methodological difference: view zakat as purely devotional or as involving a human right.
2
Identify the Hanafi response
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) said that although zakat is worship, the 'ashir has a human right (haqq al-'abd) attached in the collection. Therefore, an oath is required to protect that right.
The 'ashir is akin to a claimant; if the defendant admits, it becomes due. So the 'ashir must be given the chance to have the defendant swear, hoping for nukul.
3
Compare with other acts
Prayer and fasting have no human accuser, so no oath is needed. But zakat involves an appointed collector who has a stake.
Also, hadd of qadhf does not allow judgment by nukul, but here it is possible.
4
Consider the receipt (bara'a) issue
When claiming payment to another 'ashir, is a receipt required? The relied-upon view (following al-Jami' al-Saghir) says no, because handwriting can be forged. But Imam Muhammad required it in al-Asl.
If a receipt is produced, an oath may still be needed according to some scholars.

The author's saying: 'Whoever says the year has not completed, or he has a debt, or I have paid personally, or to another 'ashir, and he swears, he is believed except regarding livestock in paying it personally.' As for the first and second, they are denial of obligation. This passage provides detailed rules for when a person passing by the 'ashir claims that zakat is not due. Key Terms
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal to take an oath; in legal terms, can be treated as an admission of the claim.
البراءة (al-bara'a): A written receipt or discharge document proving payment of zakat.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate

Debt as a Preventer of Zakat on Trade Goods
Commentary establishing that debt prevents zakat on trade goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on debt as a preventer of zakat for trade goods and apply the ruling to both consuming and reducing debt.

Core Idea
Any debt that reduces the nisab prevents zakat—the ruling is not limited to debt that consumes the entire wealth.

This passage addresses a key nuance in zakat on trade goods: does debt that merely reduces the nisab below the threshold also prevent zakat, or only debt that consumes all wealth? Ibn Nujaym clarifies that the correct Hanafi position is inclusive—any debt that brings the net wealth below nisab removes the obligation, regardless of whether the debt is total or partial. We will trace his reasoning and the refutation of earlier restrictive views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of debt
Note the two categories: mustaghriq (debt that consumes the entire nisab) and munqis (debt that reduces but does not consume the nisab). Both are under consideration.
2
Understand the early restrictive views
Some earlier texts (Ghayat al-Bayan, al-Khabbaziyyah) suggested that only consuming debt prevents zakat, implying that reducing debt does not. Identify these positions.
One restriction tied debt to external parties; another to the debtor's testimony.
3
Analyze the illa (effective cause)
The underlying reason (illa) that debt prevents zakat is the reduction of net wealth below nisab. This applies equally to both types of debt.
The reduction itself is the preventer, not the extent of consumption.
4
Examine the refutation
Ibn Nujaym refutes the restrictions by citing al-Mi'raj and al-Quduri, which explicitly extend the preventive effect to reducing debt. There is no difference (la farq).
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that any debt reducing the nisab prevents zakat obligation. This is the operative fatwa in the Hanafi school.
This resolves a historical intra-school ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الدين فشمل المستغرق للماء والمنقص للنصاب وهو الحق وبه اندفع ما في غاية البيان من التقييد بالمحيط لما له واندفع ما في الخبازية من أن العاشر يسأله عن قدر الدين على لاصح فإن أخبره بما يستغرق النصاب يصدقه وإلا لا يصدقه اهـ لأن المنقص له مانع من الوجوب فلا فرق كما في المعراج. قوله : (وبه اندفع ما في غاية البيان الخ قال في الشرنبلالية لا يخفى ما فيه من معارضة المنطوق بالمفهوم فليتأمل اهـ. وفيه نظر لأنه لم يكتف بمفهوم كلام المصنف بل بما ينقله عن المعراج لكن عبارة المعراج بعد نقله عبارة الخبازية هكذا وقيل : ينبغي أن يصدقه فيما ينقص النصاب به لأنه لا يأخذ من المال الذي يكون أقل من النصاب لأن ما يأخذه العاشر زكاة حتى شرطت فيه شرائط الزكاة ذكره في شرح مختصر الكرخي للقدوري ا هـ . وهو صريح
Regarding debt, it includes that which consumes the water (wealth) and that which reduces the nisab, and this is the truth. And by it is repelled what is in Ghayat al-Bayan of restriction by al-Muhit regarding what he has, and by it is repelled what is in al-Khabbaziyyah that the 'Aashir asks him about the amount of debt according to the correct opinion. If he informs him of what consumes the nisab, he believes him; otherwise he does not believe him, because the reducer has a preventive from obligation, so there is no difference, as in al-Mi'raj. He said: (And by it is repelled what is in Ghayat al-Bayan etc.) He said in al-Sharnablaliyyah: It is not hidden that there is in it opposition of the explicit wording to the implication, so let one reflect. And there is consideration because he did not rely on the implication of the author's words but on what he transmits from al-Mi'raj, but the expression of al-Mi'raj after transmitting the expression of al-Khabbaziyyah is as follows: And it is said: It is proper to believe him regarding what reduces the nisab because he does not take from wealth that is less than the nisab, because what the 'Aashir takes is zakat, so the conditions of zakat are stipulated in it. It was mentioned in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Karkhi by al-Quduri. And it is explicit.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage begins by addressing the effect of debt on the zakat of trade goods. The author (Ibn Nujaym) states that the ruling concerning debt includes both debt that consumes the entire wealth (mustaghriq) and debt that only reduces the nisab (munqis). This is the correct position (huwa al-haqq). He then refutes the view in Ghayat al-Bayan that restricted the ruling to debt that is owed to external parties (al-Muhit), and also refutes the view in al-Khabbaziyyah that the 'Aashir should question the debtor about the amount: if the debtor says the debt consumes the nisab, he is believed; otherwise not. The author argues that since a reducing debt is a preventive factor (mani'), it prevents the obligation of zakat just like a consuming debt. There is no difference between the two. He supports this with al-Mi'raj. Then a marginal note from al-Sharnablaliyyah critiques this, saying there is a conflict between the explicit text and its implication, but the author defends the position, citing al-Mi'raj's transmission of al-Khabbaziyyah's statement and referencing al-Quduri's Sharh Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, which explicitly states that the 'Aashir should believe the debtor regarding any reduction. The lesson establishes that any debt that reduces the nisab prevents zakat, which is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الدين (al-dayn): A debt owed by the owner of trade goods that reduces or consumes the nisab.
النصاب (al-nisab): The minimum amount of wealth that triggers zakat obligation; for trade goods it is the value of 200 dirhams or 20 mithqals of gold.
المستغرق (al-mustaghriq): Debt that equals or exceeds the total value of the trade goods, consuming the entire nisab.
المنقص (al-munqis): Debt that reduces the nisab below the threshold, though not entirely consuming it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does reducing debt (munqis) also prevent zakat?
A) Because the debtor's confession is required.
B) Because the reduction itself is a preventer, just like consuming debt.
C) Because the 'Aashir has no authority to collect partial zakat.
D) Because the nisab is not affected by partial debt.

Key Takeaway
Any debt that reduces the net value of trade goods below the nisab prevents zakat obligation, regardless of whether the debt is total or partial.
